The Summary Answer: Existence is Mentally Added to Essence
Author gives a summary answer: existence is mentally added.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's summary response to the first argument: that the increase and inherence of existence in essence are purely mental, not external.

Core Idea
Existence is mentally added to essence, not externally.

This lesson presents the concise summary answer. Before diving into detailed refutations, the author states the core principle: the extra existence is only a mental consideration. This sets the stage for the more complex arguments to follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the summary claim.
Pay attention to the phrase 'بحسب العقل'.
2
Identify the key phrase
Notice 'اختصاص ناعت' (predicative specification) and how existence is considered a meaning with respect to essence.
3
Contrast with external inherence
The author distinguishes mental inherence from external inherence like whiteness in body. Understand why external inherence would lead to impossibilities.
External inherence would require real separation, which is problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : إما إجمالاً فهو أن زيادة الوجود على الماهية وقيامه بها إنما هو بحسب العقل، بأن يلاحظ كلاً منهما من غير ملاحظة الآخر، ويعتبر الوجود معنى له اختصاص ناعت بالماهية لا بحسب الخارج بأن يقوم الوجود بالماهية قيام البياض بالجسم، وتلزم المحالات، وإما تفصيلاً.
فعن الأول : أن قيامه بالماهية من حيث هي هي لا بالماهية المعدومة ليلزم التناقض ، ولا بالماهية الموجودة ليلزم الدور أو التسلسل .
فإن قيل : إن أريد بالماهية من حيث هي هي ما لا يكون الوجود أو العدم نفسها ولا جزءاً منها على ما قيل فغير مفيد لأن العروض كاف في لزوم المحالات، وإن أريد ما لا يكون موجوداً ولا معدوماً لا بالعروض ولا بغيره، فالتناقض فيه أظهر لأن اللاوجود نقيض الوجود بلا نزاع ولا اشتباه .
قلنا : المراد ما لا يعتبر فيه الوجود ولا العدم، وإن كان لا ينفك عن أحدهما في الخارج. فإن قيل : عدم الانفكاك عن أحدهما كاف في لزوم المحال، لأنه إن قارن العدم فيناقض، أو الوجود فيدور أو يتسلسل .
قلنا : قيام الوجود بالماهية أمر عقلي ليس كقيام البياض بالجسم ليلزم تقدمها عليه بالوجود، تقدماً ذاتياً أو زمانياً، فتلزم المحالات، بل غاية الأمر أنه يلزم تقدمها عليه بالوجود العقلي، ولا استحالة فيه لجواز أن تلاحظ وحدها من غير ملاحظة وجود خارجي أو ذهني، ويكون لها وجود ذهني لا بملاحظة العقل، فإن عدم الاعتبار غير اعتبار العدم، وإن اعتبر العقل وجودها الذهني لم يلزم التسلسل، بل ينقطع بانقطاع الاعتبار.
وأما القائلون بنفي الوجود الذهني فجوابهم الاقتصار على منع لزوم تقدم
And the answer: either in summary, it is that the increase of existence over the essence and its standing in it is only according to the mind, such that each is observed without observing the other, and existence is considered a meaning having a predicative specification with respect to the essence, not externally such that existence stands in the essence like whiteness stands in the body, leading to impossibilities; or in detail. As for the first: that its standing in the essence from the perspective of the essence itself is not in the non-existent essence so that contradiction would follow, nor in the existent essence so that circularity or regress would follow. For if it is said: if by 'essence from the perspective of the essence itself' is meant that existence or non-existence is neither identical to it nor a part of it, as is said, then it is not useful because inherence alone is sufficient for the impossibilities; and if it is meant that which is neither existent nor non-existent, neither by inherence nor otherwise, then the contradiction is more apparent because non-existence is the opposite of existence without dispute or confusion. We say: what is meant is that in which existence nor non-existence is considered, even if it does not separate from one of them in the external. For if it is said: the lack of separation from one of them is sufficient for the impossibility, because if it is accompanied by non-existence it contradicts, or by existence then circularity or regress. We say: the standing of existence in the essence is a mental matter, not like the standing of whiteness in the body so that its priority over it in existence, either essential or temporal priority, would be necessary, thus leading to impossibilities. Rather, all that follows is that it is necessary for it to have priority over it in mental existence, and there is no impossibility in that because it is permissible that it be observed alone without observing external or mental existence, and it has mental existence not by the mind's observation. Indeed, non-consideration is not the same as considering non-existence. And if the mind considers its mental existence, regress does not follow, but rather it is cut off by the cutting off of consideration. As for those who deny mental existence, their answer is limited to preventing the necessity of priority...
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage provides the response to the objections. First, a summary answer: the increase and inherence are only mental, not external. Then a detailed answer for the first argument: existence stands in the essence from the perspective of the essence itself, not as inhering in an already non-existent or already existent essence. An objection follows: what does 'essence itself' mean? If it means essence without existence or non-existence as constituents, that is still problematic because inherence alone implies the essence must be either existent or non-existent. The author responds: the essence considered in itself neither includes existence nor non-existence, though it cannot be separated from one in reality. The priority involved is only mental, not temporal or essential, so regress is avoided. Mental consideration cuts off the regress, and non-consideration is not the same as considering non-existence. Finally, for those who deny mental existence altogether, the answer is simply to reject the necessity of priority, but the text is cut off.

Key Terms
بحسب العقل
اختصاص ناعت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the existence-essence distinction is real and external, like two separate things.
Correction: This corrects that it is only mental, not a real external separation.

Checkpoint
According to the summary answer, where does existence inhere in the essence?
A) In the external world
B) Only in the mind
C) As a physical property
D) Both externally and mentally

Key Takeaway
Existence is only mentally considered to inhere in essence, not externally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your existence is not an external property you possess but a mental consideration of your essence, what does that reveal about your absolute dependence on the One whose existence is His essence? Do you truly live as if your very being is given to you at every moment? How will you remind yourself of this dependency today?
?
The author warns that if existence were an external accident like whiteness, absurdities would follow. Reflect on the 'absurdities' in your own life when you treat your existence as something you own and control. In what specific situation do you need to acknowledge that you are not the source of your own being? What concrete act of humility can you perform to affirm that your existence is a gift?
?
Consider the phrase 'بحسب العقل' — according to the mind. Your mind can separate existence from essence, but in reality they are not separate. How does this mental exercise change the way you view your own identity? Are you willing to let go of the illusion that you exist independently from God? What is holding you back from that surrender?

Detailed Refutation: Existence Inheres in Essence as Such
Author provides a detailed refutation of the first argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed argument that existence inheres in essence from the perspective of the essence itself, and how objections about priority are resolved through mental consideration.

Core Idea
Existence inheres in essence as such, not in a pre-existing subject.

This lesson delves into the detailed refutation of the first argument. The author explains that existence stands in the essence from the perspective of the essence itself, avoiding the pitfalls of circularity or regress. The discussion addresses objections about the meaning of 'essence itself' and the nature of priority.

Guided Path
1
Read the detailed refutation
Follow the author's argument step by step from the first point to the final note on mental existence.
The passage is dense; take it slowly.
2
Identify the first point
Existence stands in essence from the perspective of essence itself, not in a non-existent or existent essence.
3
Follow the objection and response
Understand the clarification about 'essence itself' meaning neither existence nor non-existence is considered.
Non-consideration is not the same as considering non-existence.
4
Grasp the second objection and response
The priority involved is mental, not temporal or essential, so regress is avoided.
Mental priority is possible because the mind can cut off consideration.
5
Consider the final note on mental existence
For deniers of mental existence, the argument still holds by simply denying the necessity of priority.
The text is cut off here, but the point is clear.

?

Are there truths about yourself that you avoid because they seem contradictory when considered with your existence? The First Group's Proof and Its Limits
Sanusi critiques the first group's proof for existence being additional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument of those who claim existence is additional to essence, and why their proof only shows a conceptual difference, not an accident.

Core Idea
The first group's proof shows conceptual distinction, not external addition.

Sanusi begins by examining the proofs of those who hold that existence is additional to essence. He shows that their arguments only demonstrate a difference in concept, not that existence is an accident inhering in the thing. Even if such strong claims appear in the works of great scholars like al-Razi, the intellect does not accept them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp Sanusi's critique of the first group's proof.
Notice that Sanusi uses 'لا يفيد سوى' to limit what the proof actually shows.
2
Identify the claim and the limitation
Identify what the first group claims (existence is additional) and what Sanusi says their proof actually shows (conceptual difference only).
The proof does not indicate that existence is an accident like whiteness in a body.
3
Reflect on why the intellect rejects the stronger claim
Consider why Sanusi says the intellect does not accept the claim that existence is an accident, even if made by authorities.
This shows that rational evidence takes precedence over authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول. أدلة القائلين بأن وجود الشيء زائد عليه لا يفيد سوى أن ليس المفهوم من وجود الشيء هو المفهوم من ذلك الشيء من غير دلالة على أنه عرض قائم به قيام العرض بالمحل . فإن هذا مما لا يقبله العقل وإن وقع في كلام الإمام وغيره، وأدلة القائلين بأن وجود الشيء نفس ذاته لا يفيد سوى أن ليس للشيء هوية، ولعارضه المسمى بالوجود هوية أخرى قائمة بالأولى بحيث يجتمعان اجتماع البياض والجسم من غير دلالة على أن المفهوم من وجود الشيء هو المفهوم من الشيء. فإن هذا بديهي البطلان فإذا لا يظهر من كلام الفريقين ولا يتصور من المنصف، خلاف في أن الوجود زائد على الماهية ذهناً، أي عند العقل وبحسب المفهوم والتصور بمعنى أن للعقل أن يلاحظ الوجود دون الماهية ، والماهية دون الوجود لا عيناً، أي بحسب الذات والهوية بأن يكون لكل منهما هوية متميزة يقوم أحدهما بالأخرى كبياض الجسم، فعند تحرير المبحث وبيان أن المراد الزيادة في التصور أو في الهوية يرتفع النزاع بين الفريقين، ويظهر أن القول يكون اشتراك الوجود لفظياً، بمعنى أن المفهوم من الوجود المضاف إلى الإنسان غير المفهوم من المضاف إلى الفرس، ولا اشتراك بينهما في مفهوم الكون مكابرة ومخالفة لبديهة العقل
So we say: The proofs of those who say that the existence of a thing is additional to it only indicate that the concept of the existence of a thing is not the concept of that thing, without indicating that it is an accident subsisting in it like the subsistence of an accident in its subject. For this is not accepted by the intellect, even if it occurs in the speech of al-Imam (al-Razi) and others. And the proofs of those who say that the existence of a thing is its own essence only indicate that the thing does not have an essence, and its accident called existence has another essence subsisting in the first, such that they combine like the combination of whiteness and body, without indicating that the concept of existence is the concept of the thing. For this is self-evidently false. Thus, it is not apparent from the speech of the two groups—and cannot be imagined from a fair-minded person—a dispute about existence being additional to the essence mentally, i.e., with respect to the intellect and according to concept and conception, meaning that the intellect can observe existence without the essence, and essence without existence; not externally, i.e., according to self and identity such that each has a distinct identity and one subsists through the other like whiteness of the body. So when the inquiry is refined and it is clarified that what is meant is increase in concept or in identity, the dispute between the two groups is resolved, and it becomes clear that the claim that existence is a verbal homonym—meaning that the concept of existence attributed to a human is different from that attributed to a horse, and there is no commonality between them in the concept of 'being in the world'—is obstinacy and contrary to the primary intuition of reason.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage analyzes the proofs of both sides and proposes a resolution. The author first examines the argument of those who say existence is additional: their proofs only show that the concept of existence differs from the concept of the thing, not that existence is an accident inhering in the thing like whiteness in a body. Such a claim is intellectually unacceptable, even if found in the works of al-Imam (al-Razi) and others. Similarly, the proofs of those who say existence is identical to essence only show that there is no separate identity for existence—it is not a second identity subsisting in the first—but they do not prove that the concept of existence is the same as the concept of the thing; that is self-evidently false. The author then states that the real dispute is not about increase in identity (external reality) but about increase in concept (mental distinction). No fair-minded person would deny that existence is conceptually additional to essence: the mind can conceive of existence without essence and vice versa. The dispute dissolves when it is clarified that the increase is in conception, not in identity. Then the author warns against the view that existence is a pure verbal homonym—e.g., 'existence' means something completely different when applied to a human vs. a horse, with no common meaning of 'being in the world'—calling that obstinacy and contrary to reason.

Key Terms
زيادة
عرض
مفهوم
عقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That existence being additional means existence is an accident like whiteness in a body.
Correction: The proof only shows conceptual difference, not that existence is an accident. The intellect rejects the stronger claim.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what do the proofs of those who say existence is additional actually demonstrate?
A) That existence is an accident
B) That the concept of existence differs from the concept of the thing
C) That existence is identical to essence
D) That existence is a verbal homonym

Key Takeaway
The first group's proof only establishes a conceptual distinction, not an external accident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever confused a concept in your mind with an external reality? How might that lead to error in your belief about Allah's attributes?
?
The text says the intellect does not accept existence as an accident. When you think about your own existence, do you feel it as a separate 'thing' added to you? How does this lesson correct that tendency?
?
If a scholar like al-Razi can be mistaken, what does that tell you about the necessity of verifying proofs yourself? In what area of your faith are you relying on authority without rational verification?

The Second Group's Proof and Its Limits
Sanusi critiques the second group's proof for existence being identical to essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument of those who claim existence is identical to essence, and why their proof only shows no separate identity, not conceptual identity.

Core Idea
The second group's proof shows no separate identity, not that the concepts are the same.

Sanusi then turns to the second group, who argue that existence is the very essence of a thing. He critiques their proof, showing that it only demonstrates that existence does not have a separate identity from essence, not that the concept of existence is identical to the concept of the thing. He declares that the latter claim is self-evidently false.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage for the second group's argument and Sanusi's response.
Notice the phrase 'بديهي البطلان' which indicates a strong rejection.
2
Distinguish between identity and concept
Understand that the proof only denies a separate identity for existence, but does not prove that the concept of existence is the same as the concept of essence.
Sanusi argues that claiming conceptual identity is self-evidently false.
3
Appreciate the balance
See how Sanusi treats both groups equally, showing that each oversteps the implications of its proof.
This prepares for the resolution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول. أدلة القائلين بأن وجود الشيء زائد عليه لا يفيد سوى أن ليس المفهوم من وجود الشيء هو المفهوم من ذلك الشيء من غير دلالة على أنه عرض قائم به قيام العرض بالمحل . فإن هذا مما لا يقبله العقل وإن وقع في كلام الإمام وغيره، وأدلة القائلين بأن وجود الشيء نفس ذاته لا يفيد سوى أن ليس للشيء هوية، ولعارضه المسمى بالوجود هوية أخرى قائمة بالأولى بحيث يجتمعان اجتماع البياض والجسم من غير دلالة على أن المفهوم من وجود الشيء هو المفهوم من الشيء. فإن هذا بديهي البطلان فإذا لا يظهر من كلام الفريقين ولا يتصور من المنصف، خلاف في أن الوجود زائد على الماهية ذهناً، أي عند العقل وبحسب المفهوم والتصور بمعنى أن للعقل أن يلاحظ الوجود دون الماهية ، والماهية دون الوجود لا عيناً، أي بحسب الذات والهوية بأن يكون لكل منهما هوية متميزة يقوم أحدهما بالأخرى كبياض الجسم، فعند تحرير المبحث وبيان أن المراد الزيادة في التصور أو في الهوية يرتفع النزاع بين الفريقين، ويظهر أن القول يكون اشتراك الوجود لفظياً، بمعنى أن المفهوم من الوجود المضاف إلى الإنسان غير المفهوم من المضاف إلى الفرس، ولا اشتراك بينهما في مفهوم الكون مكابرة ومخالفة لبديهة العقل
So we say: The proofs of those who say that the existence of a thing is additional to it only indicate that the concept of the existence of a thing is not the concept of that thing, without indicating that it is an accident subsisting in it like the subsistence of an accident in its subject. For this is not accepted by the intellect, even if it occurs in the speech of al-Imam (al-Razi) and others. And the proofs of those who say that the existence of a thing is its own essence only indicate that the thing does not have an essence, and its accident called existence has another essence subsisting in the first, such that they combine like the combination of whiteness and body, without indicating that the concept of existence is the concept of the thing. For this is self-evidently false. Thus, it is not apparent from the speech of the two groups—and cannot be imagined from a fair-minded person—a dispute about existence being additional to the essence mentally, i.e., with respect to the intellect and according to concept and conception, meaning that the intellect can observe existence without the essence, and essence without existence; not externally, i.e., according to self and identity such that each has a distinct identity and one subsists through the other like whiteness of the body. So when the inquiry is refined and it is clarified that what is meant is increase in concept or in identity, the dispute between the two groups is resolved, and it becomes clear that the claim that existence is a verbal homonym—meaning that the concept of existence attributed to a human is different from that attributed to a horse, and there is no commonality between them in the concept of 'being in the world'—is obstinacy and contrary to the primary intuition of reason.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage analyzes the proofs of both sides and proposes a resolution. The author first examines the argument of those who say existence is additional: their proofs only show that the concept of existence differs from the concept of the thing, not that existence is an accident inhering in the thing like whiteness in a body. Such a claim is intellectually unacceptable, even if found in the works of al-Imam (al-Razi) and others. Similarly, the proofs of those who say existence is identical to essence only show that there is no separate identity for existence—it is not a second identity subsisting in the first—but they do not prove that the concept of existence is the same as the concept of the thing; that is self-evidently false. The author then states that the real dispute is not about increase in identity (external reality) but about increase in concept (mental distinction). No fair-minded person would deny that existence is conceptually additional to essence: the mind can conceive of existence without essence and vice versa. The dispute dissolves when it is clarified that the increase is in conception, not in identity. Then the author warns against the view that existence is a pure verbal homonym—e.g., 'existence' means something completely different when applied to a human vs. a horse, with no common meaning of 'being in the world'—calling that obstinacy and contrary to reason.

Key Terms
نفس
ذات
هوية
بديهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That existence being identical to essence means the concept of existence is the same as the concept of the thing.
Correction: The proof only shows no separate identity, not that the concepts are identical.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi say about the claim that the concept of existence is the same as the concept of the thing?
A) It is proven by the second group
B) It is self-evidently false
C) It is accepted by the intellect
D) It is a matter of dispute

Key Takeaway
The second group's proof only denies a separate identity, not conceptual identity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because two things are inseparable in reality (like existence and essence), their meanings must be identical? How does Sanusi's distinction between separate identity and conceptual identity challenge your tendency to conflate distinctions in your understanding of Allah's attributes? What is one area of your faith where you need to hold onto conceptual distinctions instead of flattening them for simplicity?
?
If the concept of existence and the concept of a thing are not identical, then your mental grasp of Allah's existence is not the same as His essence. Does this humble you? How does this affect the way you speak about Allah—do you speak as if your concepts capture Him fully? Commit to one change in how you describe Allah, acknowledging the gap between concept and reality.
?
The proof that existence is not a separate entity could lead to a lazy assumption that the concepts are the same. Where else in your religious thinking have you jumped from 'not separate' to 'identical'? (e.g., attributes of Allah, predestination and free will). Will you now pause to examine whether your conceptual categories truly align with the sources, or merely collapse distinctions for comfort?

Resolving the Dispute: Conceptual vs. Real Increase
Sanusi resolves the dispute and refutes the verbal homonym claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the dispute between the two groups is resolved by distinguishing conceptual increase from real identity, and why the verbal homonym view is invalid.

Core Idea
The dispute dissolves when we see it is about conceptual increase, not identity.

Sanusi now presents the resolution. He explains that no fair-minded person disputes that existence is conceptually additional to essence—the mind can consider existence without essence and vice versa. The real disagreement is only about whether this addition is mental or real. Once this is clarified, the dispute ends. He also warns against the extreme view that 'existence' is a pure verbal homonym with no common meaning, calling it obstinacy against reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the resolution passage
Read the Arabic passage where Sanusi explains the mental increase and the dissolution of the dispute.
Notice the terms 'ذهناً' and 'عيناً' which are key.
2
Understand the distinction
Grasp that 'mental increase' means the mind can separate existence from essence, while 'real increase' would mean two distinct identities like whiteness and body.
The first group wants real increase; the second denies even mental increase. Sanusi says everyone accepts mental increase.
3
Reflect on the verbal homonym warning
Consider why denying any common meaning of 'existence' (like 'being in the world') is contrary to reason.
This shows the importance of universal concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول. أدلة القائلين بأن وجود الشيء زائد عليه لا يفيد سوى أن ليس المفهوم من وجود الشيء هو المفهوم من ذلك الشيء من غير دلالة على أنه عرض قائم به قيام العرض بالمحل . فإن هذا مما لا يقبله العقل وإن وقع في كلام الإمام وغيره، وأدلة القائلين بأن وجود الشيء نفس ذاته لا يفيد سوى أن ليس للشيء هوية، ولعارضه المسمى بالوجود هوية أخرى قائمة بالأولى بحيث يجتمعان اجتماع البياض والجسم من غير دلالة على أن المفهوم من وجود الشيء هو المفهوم من الشيء. فإن هذا بديهي البطلان فإذا لا يظهر من كلام الفريقين ولا يتصور من المنصف، خلاف في أن الوجود زائد على الماهية ذهناً، أي عند العقل وبحسب المفهوم والتصور بمعنى أن للعقل أن يلاحظ الوجود دون الماهية ، والماهية دون الوجود لا عيناً، أي بحسب الذات والهوية بأن يكون لكل منهما هوية متميزة يقوم أحدهما بالأخرى كبياض الجسم، فعند تحرير المبحث وبيان أن المراد الزيادة في التصور أو في الهوية يرتفع النزاع بين الفريقين، ويظهر أن القول يكون اشتراك الوجود لفظياً، بمعنى أن المفهوم من الوجود المضاف إلى الإنسان غير المفهوم من المضاف إلى الفرس، ولا اشتراك بينهما في مفهوم الكون مكابرة ومخالفة لبديهة العقل
So we say: The proofs of those who say that the existence of a thing is additional to it only indicate that the concept of the existence of a thing is not the concept of that thing, without indicating that it is an accident subsisting in it like the subsistence of an accident in its subject. For this is not accepted by the intellect, even if it occurs in the speech of al-Imam (al-Razi) and others. And the proofs of those who say that the existence of a thing is its own essence only indicate that the thing does not have an essence, and its accident called existence has another essence subsisting in the first, such that they combine like the combination of whiteness and body, without indicating that the concept of existence is the concept of the thing. For this is self-evidently false. Thus, it is not apparent from the speech of the two groups—and cannot be imagined from a fair-minded person—a dispute about existence being additional to the essence mentally, i.e., with respect to the intellect and according to concept and conception, meaning that the intellect can observe existence without the essence, and essence without existence; not externally, i.e., according to self and identity such that each has a distinct identity and one subsists through the other like whiteness of the body. So when the inquiry is refined and it is clarified that what is meant is increase in concept or in identity, the dispute between the two groups is resolved, and it becomes clear that the claim that existence is a verbal homonym—meaning that the concept of existence attributed to a human is different from that attributed to a horse, and there is no commonality between them in the concept of 'being in the world'—is obstinacy and contrary to the primary intuition of reason.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage analyzes the proofs of both sides and proposes a resolution. The author first examines the argument of those who say existence is additional: their proofs only show that the concept of existence differs from the concept of the thing, not that existence is an accident inhering in the thing like whiteness in a body. Such a claim is intellectually unacceptable, even if found in the works of al-Imam (al-Razi) and others. Similarly, the proofs of those who say existence is identical to essence only show that there is no separate identity for existence—it is not a second identity subsisting in the first—but they do not prove that the concept of existence is the same as the concept of the thing; that is self-evidently false. The author then states that the real dispute is not about increase in identity (external reality) but about increase in concept (mental distinction). No fair-minded person would deny that existence is conceptually additional to essence: the mind can conceive of existence without essence and vice versa. The dispute dissolves when it is clarified that the increase is in conception, not in identity. Then the author warns against the view that existence is a pure verbal homonym—e.g., 'existence' means something completely different when applied to a human vs. a horse, with no common meaning of 'being in the world'—calling that obstinacy and contrary to reason.

Objection and Answer on Universal Existence and Multiple Existences
Objection that universal existence as accident yields multiple existences and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that universal existence as an accident would imply multiple existences in each thing, and the response that the shares of the universal are merely mental forms, thus avoiding external duplication.

Core Idea
Universal existence does not cause multiple existences because its 'shares' exist only in the mind, not in reality.

This passage presents a critical objection to the view that universal existence is an accident of particular existences. If it were, each particular would have a share of this universal, leading to multiple existences. The answer deftly shows that these shares are merely mental distinctions with no external reality, preserving the unity of existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage (from 'فإن قلت' to 'يكذبه العقل والحس') and its translation. The objector claims that if universal existence were an accident, each thing would have two existences.
Notice the example of snow having two whitenesses is used to show absurdity.
2
Identify the key terms
Look for terms like 'مطلق', 'عارض', 'حصة', 'مفهوم الكون', and 'الوجود الخاص'. Understand how they relate to the objection.
These terms are central to the argument.
3
Understand the answer
Read the answer (from 'قلت لا نزاع' to the end). The responder argues that the shares are only mentally distinct, not externally real, and that the dispute is about whether God has a quiddity.
The key is the distinction between mental and external existence.
4
Grasp the role of tashkīk
Notice that after affirming gradation (tashkīk), the difference of existences is necessary but only in the intellect, not externally.
This supports the answer by showing that mental distinction does not imply external multiplicity.
5
Review the conclusion
The answer concludes that the shares are mere mental forms, so no two existences follow. Re-read the final sentence to solidify this.
The example of snow and whiteness is directly refuted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قال : فإن قلت لو كان المطلق عارضاً لها لكان في كل منها حصة من مفهوم الكون كما هو شأن الأعراض العامة فتكون الحصة من مفهوم الكون زائداً على ما هو حقيقة الواجب كما في الممكنات، ويلزم فيه وجودان معروض وعارض. وفي الممكن وجودان وماهية ، وعلى هذا في الثلج بیاضان ، وهذا مما يكذبه العقل والحس . قلت لا نزاع لهم في زيادة الحصة من مفهوم الكون على الوجود الخاص الذي هو حقيقة الواجب، إذ لا فرق بين مفهوم الكون والحصص منه إلا بمجرد اعتبار الإضافة، وإنما نزاعهم في أن تكون له ماهية يزيد عليها الوجود الخاص في التعقل وثبوته وبعد القول بالتشكيك فمغايرة الوجودات المخصص من مفهوم الكون ضروري لكن بحسب العقل دون الخارج، لما تقرر من اتحاد الموضوع والمحمول بحسب الذات، على أن الحصص من مفهوم العام صور عقلية لا تحقق لها في الأعيان، فلا يلزم للممكن وجودان ولا في الأبيض بياضان .)
He said: For if you said that if the absolute (al-mutlaq) were an accident (āriḍ) of them, then in each of them there would be a share (ḥiṣṣa) from the concept of existence (mafhūm al-kawn) as is the case with general accidents, so the share from the concept of existence would be additional to what is the reality of the Necessary Being (ḥaqīqat al-wājib) as in contingent beings (mumkināt). And two existences would follow in Him: a substrate (maʻrūḍ) and an accident (ʻāriḍ). And in the contingent there would be two existences and a quiddity (māhiyya). On this basis, in snow there would be two whitenesses, and this is belied by reason and sense. I said: There is no dispute among them about the share from the concept of existence being additional to the particular existence (al-wujūd al-khāṣṣ) which is the reality of the Necessary Being, for there is no difference between the concept of existence and the shares from it except by mere consideration of relation (iʻtibār al-iḍāfa). Rather their dispute is whether He has a quiddity to which the particular existence is additional in intellect and in its subsistence. And after the affirmation of gradation (tashkīk), the difference of the existences specified from the concept of existence is necessary, but according to the intellect (al-ʻaql) not according to the external world (al-khārij), because it has been established that the subject and predicate are united in essence (bi-ḥasab al-dhāt). Moreover, the shares from the concept of the universal are mental forms (ṣuwar ʻaqliyya) having no realization in external individuals (aʻyān). So for a contingent there does not necessarily follow two existences, nor in the white thing two whitenesses.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer regarding the problem of universal existence (al-wujūd al-muṭlaq) and its application to particular existences. The objector argues: if universal existence were an accident (ʻāriḍ) of each particular existence, then each particular would have a share (ḥiṣṣa) from the concept of existence (mafhūm al-kawn), as is the case with general accidents like whiteness. This share would be additional to the particular existence, just as in contingent beings existence is additional to quiddity. Consequently, in the Necessary Being there would be two existences: one as substrate and one as accident. Similarly, in a contingent there would be two existences and a quiddity. For example, in snow there would be two whitenesses: the particular whiteness of snow and the universal whiteness as a share. But this is impossible by reason and sense. The answer denies that the share is an additional external reality. It states that no one disputes that the share from the concept of existence is additional to the particular existence that is the reality of the Necessary Being, because the difference between the concept and its shares is only by mental consideration of relation (iʻtibār al-iḍāfa). The real dispute is whether the Necessary Being has a quiddity to which existence is added. With the doctrine of gradation (tashkīk), the distinction between existences is necessary but only in the intellect, not in the external world, because the subject and predicate are the same in essence. The shares of the universal are merely mental forms with no external reality. Hence, no duplication of existences occurs.

عارض
حصة
مفهوم الكون
الوجود الخاص
تشكيك
صور عقلية
اتحاد الموضوع والمحمول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That universal existence being an accident of particulars would create multiple external existences.
Correction: The shares of the universal exist only in the mind, not in the external world, so no external multiplicity arises.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in this passage, why does the share from universal existence not imply two external existences in a contingent thing?
A) Because the share is identical to the particular existence.
B) Because the share is a mental form with no external reality.
C) Because the particular existence already includes the universal.
D) Because the universal is not an accident but a substance.

Key Takeaway
The objection that universal existence causes multiple existences is refuted by showing that its shares are merely mental forms, preserving the external unity of each existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objection argues that if God were described by a universal accident, He would have multiple existences—a absurdity. Reflect on your own concept of God: Are there any attributes you mentally add to Him that are only in your mind, making Him seem complex? How can you purify your understanding to affirm His absolute simplicity?
?
The answer clarifies that the 'shares' of existence are merely mental forms with no external reality. Think of a time you confused a mental label (like 'success' or 'failure') with the actual external reality. How does this distinction challenge you to separate what is in your mind from what is truly real in your relationship with Allah?
?
Gradation (tashkīk) means existence is one but varies in perfection intellectually. How does this affect your view of your own spiritual station? Are you content with a lower 'share' of faith when you could strive for more, knowing that the external reality of existence is one? What will you change today?

Disagreement on Particular vs Universal Existence
Detailed commentary on the particular vs universal debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate on whether existence is particular or universal, and why the author affirms it is universal.

Core Idea
Existence is universal; individual existences are its instances.

After listing eight disagreements about existence, the author begins his detailed commentary with the first: is existence particular or universal? Some claim it is genuinely particular with no multiplicity, but the author asserts the truth that existence is universal. Let's examine the arguments and the author's stance.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage that begins with 'فمنها اختلافهم في أنه جزئي أو كلي' and includes the two views and the author's conclusion.
Focus on how the author uses the phrase 'والحق أنه كلي'.
2
Understand the particularist view
Some say existence is genuine particular with no multiplicity; multiplicity is only through relations, like saying 'the existence of Zayd' is like 'the god of Zayd'.
This view treats existence as unique to each entity.
3
Identify the author's truth
The author states the truth is that existence is universal and existences (individual instances) are its individuals. This means existence is a common concept applied to many.
This aligns with the idea that existence is a universal predicate.
4
Reflect on the debate's significance
The debate affects how we talk about existence in theology: if existence is particular, each thing's existence is entirely distinct; if universal, existence is shared across all existents.
The author's view favors a shared concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اختلاف العقلاء في الوجود هو جزئي أو كلي؟ (قال) وما أعجب حال الوجود أطبقوا على أنه بديهي لا أعرف منه، ثم اختلفوا في أنه جزئي أو كلي، واجب أو ممكن عرض أو لا عرض ولا جوهر موجود أو اعتباري لا تحقق له في الأعيان أو واسطة، وأفراده عين الماهيات أو زائدة ولفظه مشترك، أو متواطىء، أو مشكك. يتعجب من اختلافات العقلاء في أحوال الوجود ومع اتفاقهم على أنه أعرف الأشياء أن الغالب من حال الشيء أن تتبع ذاته في الجلاء والخفاء، فمنها اختلافهم في أنه جزئي أو كلي. فقيل: جزئي حقيقي لا تعدد فيه أصلاً. وإنما في الموجودات بواسطة الإضافات، حتى إن قولنا وجود زيد أو وجود عمرو بمنزلة قولنا: إله زيد وإله عمرو. والحق أنه كلي والوجودات أفراده ومنها اختلافهم في أنه واجب أو ممكن. فقد ذهب جمع كثير من المتأخرين إلى أنه واجب على ما ذكرنا، وذلك هو الضلال البعيد. ومنها اختلافهم في أنه عرض أو جوهر، أو ليس بعرض ولا جوهر، لكونهما من أقسام الممكن الموجود وهذا هو الحق. وفي كلام الإمام ما يشعر أنه عرض وبه صرح جمع كثير من المتكلمين، وهو بعيد جداً لأن العرض ما لا يتقوم بنفسه بل بمحله المستغني عنه في تقدمه . . ولا يتصور استغناء شيء
The disagreement of the rational ones about existence: is it particular or universal? (He said:) And how amazing is the state of existence! They agreed that it is self-evident, of which nothing is more known, then they disagreed about whether it is particular or universal, necessary or possible, accident or not accident nor substance, existent or mental having no realization in external entities, or an intermediate, and its individuals are identical to quiddities or additional, and its word is equivocal, or univocal, or analogical. He marvels at the disagreements of the rational ones about the states of existence, despite their agreement that it is the most known of things, that the usual state of a thing is that its essence is followed in clarity and obscurity. Among them is their disagreement about whether it is particular or universal. For it was said: It is genuinely particular, having no multiplicity at all. Rather, multiplicity in existents is through relations, so that our saying 'the existence of Zayd' or 'the existence of Amr' is like our saying 'the god of Zayd' and 'the god of Amr'. And the truth is that it is universal and existences are its individuals. And among them is their disagreement about whether it is necessary or possible. For a large group of later scholars have held that it is necessary as we mentioned, and that is far misguidance. And among them is their disagreement about whether it is an accident or a substance, or is neither accident nor substance, because the two are divisions of possible existence, and this is the truth. And in the words of Imam al-Haramayn there is something suggesting it is an accident, and a large group of theologians explicitly stated it, but it is very far-fetched because an accident is that which does not subsist by itself but rather by its locus which is independent of it in its precedence... and the independence of anything is not conceivable.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage begins with the author’s quotation from the original text expressing wonder that existence, despite being the most self-evident concept, is subject to so many disagreements. It then lists eight points of contention: (1) whether existence is particular or universal, (2) necessary or contingent, (3) an accident, (4) not an accident nor a substance, (5) existent or mental, (6) an intermediate, (7) whether its individuals are identical to quiddities or additional, and (8) whether the word 'existence' is equivocal, univocal, or analogical. The commentary then picks three of these disagreements in detail. First, regarding particularity vs. universality: some claim existence is genuinely particular with no multiplicity, but the author asserts the truth is that it is universal and individual existences are its instances. Second, regarding necessity vs. contingency: many later philosophers concluded existence is necessary (i.e., identical with the Necessary Being), which the author sharply condemns as far misguidance. Third, regarding whether existence is an accident or substance: some say it is an accident, others substance, but the author states the truth is that it is neither, since both accident and substance are categories of possible existent things. He notes that Imam al-Haramayn’s words lean toward accident, and many theologians explicitly stated it, but this is far-fetched because accidents subsist in a subject that is ontologically prior and independent—yet existence cannot be thus dependent. The passage breaks off mid-argument.

Key Terms
جزئي
كلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Existence is genuinely particular and has no multiplicity at all.
Correction: The author asserts that existence is universal and individual existences are its individuals, meaning existence is a shared concept.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what analogy do proponents of the particularist view use?
A) The existence of Zayd is like the whiteness of Zayd
B) The existence of Zayd is like the god of Zayd
C) The existence of Zayd is like the humanity of Zayd
D) The existence of Zayd is like the substance of Zayd

Key Takeaway
Existence is a universal concept; individual existences are its instances, not entirely distinct entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that existence is universal and you are just one instance of it. Does this humble you when you consider that your existence is no more special than that of a beggar or an enemy? How will you change your interactions with others based on this shared reality?
?
If the universal concept of existence is one, then your life is not entirely your own. What does that mean for how you use your time and resources? Are you living as if your existence is a separate possession, or as a part of a greater whole?
?
The particular view says 'existence of Zayd' is like 'god of Zayd'—each distinct. The truth says existence is universal and instances are individuals. Do you fear losing your individuality if you accept the universal view? Or can you find peace in being a meaningful instance of a greater reality?

Disagreement on Necessary vs Possible Existence
Commentary on the necessary vs possible debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the controversy over whether existence is necessary or possible, and why the author condemns the view that existence is necessary.

Core Idea
Existence is not necessary; claiming it is necessary is far misguidance.

The second disagreement the author examines is whether existence is necessary or possible. Some later scholars argued that existence is necessary (i.e., identical with the Necessary Being). The author sharply rejects this, calling it far misguidance. Let's explore why this view is problematic.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage from 'ومنها اختلافهم في أنه واجب أو ممكن' to 'وذلك هو الضلال البعيد'.
Notice the strong language: 'ضلال بعيد'.
2
Understand the view of late scholars
A large group of later scholars held that existence is necessary, meaning it is identical with God or a necessary attribute.
The author previously mentioned something similar in his own context?
3
Grasp the author's condemnation
The author calls this view 'far misguidance' (الضلال البعيد), indicating it is a serious theological error.
This likely stems from blurring the distinction between Creator and creation.
4
Reflect on theological implications
If existence is necessary, then all existents would be necessary, negating contingency and the need for a Creator. This contradicts Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اختلاف العقلاء في الوجود هو جزئي أو كلي؟ (قال) وما أعجب حال الوجود أطبقوا على أنه بديهي لا أعرف منه، ثم اختلفوا في أنه جزئي أو كلي، واجب أو ممكن عرض أو لا عرض ولا جوهر موجود أو اعتباري لا تحقق له في الأعيان أو واسطة، وأفراده عين الماهيات أو زائدة ولفظه مشترك، أو متواطىء، أو مشكك. يتعجب من اختلافات العقلاء في أحوال الوجود ومع اتفاقهم على أنه أعرف الأشياء أن الغالب من حال الشيء أن تتبع ذاته في الجلاء والخفاء، فمنها اختلافهم في أنه جزئي أو كلي. فقيل: جزئي حقيقي لا تعدد فيه أصلاً. وإنما في الموجودات بواسطة الإضافات، حتى إن قولنا وجود زيد أو وجود عمرو بمنزلة قولنا: إله زيد وإله عمرو. والحق أنه كلي والوجودات أفراده ومنها اختلافهم في أنه واجب أو ممكن. فقد ذهب جمع كثير من المتأخرين إلى أنه واجب على ما ذكرنا، وذلك هو الضلال البعيد. ومنها اختلافهم في أنه عرض أو جوهر، أو ليس بعرض ولا جوهر، لكونهما من أقسام الممكن الموجود وهذا هو الحق. وفي كلام الإمام ما يشعر أنه عرض وبه صرح جمع كثير من المتكلمين، وهو بعيد جداً لأن العرض ما لا يتقوم بنفسه بل بمحله المستغني عنه في تقدمه . . ولا يتصور استغناء شيء
The disagreement of the rational ones about existence: is it particular or universal? (He said:) And how amazing is the state of existence! They agreed that it is self-evident, of which nothing is more known, then they disagreed about whether it is particular or universal, necessary or possible, accident or not accident nor substance, existent or mental having no realization in external entities, or an intermediate, and its individuals are identical to quiddities or additional, and its word is equivocal, or univocal, or analogical. He marvels at the disagreements of the rational ones about the states of existence, despite their agreement that it is the most known of things, that the usual state of a thing is that its essence is followed in clarity and obscurity. Among them is their disagreement about whether it is particular or universal. For it was said: It is genuinely particular, having no multiplicity at all. Rather, multiplicity in existents is through relations, so that our saying 'the existence of Zayd' or 'the existence of Amr' is like our saying 'the god of Zayd' and 'the god of Amr'. And the truth is that it is universal and existences are its individuals. And among them is their disagreement about whether it is necessary or possible. For a large group of later scholars have held that it is necessary as we mentioned, and that is far misguidance. And among them is their disagreement about whether it is an accident or a substance, or is neither accident nor substance, because the two are divisions of possible existence, and this is the truth. And in the words of Imam al-Haramayn there is something suggesting it is an accident, and a large group of theologians explicitly stated it, but it is very far-fetched because an accident is that which does not subsist by itself but rather by its locus which is independent of it in its precedence... and the independence of anything is not conceivable.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage begins with the author’s quotation from the original text expressing wonder that existence, despite being the most self-evident concept, is subject to so many disagreements. It then lists eight points of contention: (1) whether existence is particular or universal, (2) necessary or contingent, (3) an accident, (4) not an accident nor a substance, (5) existent or mental, (6) an intermediate, (7) whether its individuals are identical to quiddities or additional, and (8) whether the word 'existence' is equivocal, univocal, or analogical. The commentary then picks three of these disagreements in detail. First, regarding particularity vs. universality: some claim existence is genuinely particular with no multiplicity, but the author asserts the truth is that it is universal and individual existences are its instances. Second, regarding necessity vs. contingency: many later philosophers concluded existence is necessary (i.e., identical with the Necessary Being), which the author sharply condemns as far misguidance. Third, regarding whether existence is an accident or substance: some say it is an accident, others substance, but the author states the truth is that it is neither, since both accident and substance are categories of possible existent things. He notes that Imam al-Haramayn’s words lean toward accident, and many theologians explicitly stated it, but this is far-fetched because accidents subsist in a subject that is ontologically prior and independent—yet existence cannot be thus dependent. The passage breaks off mid-argument.

واجب
ممكن

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Existence is necessary (identical with the Necessary Being).
Correction: The author condemns this as far misguidance; existence is possible (contingent) for creatures.

Checkpoint
How does the author describe the view that existence is necessary?
A) A plausible opinion
B) Far misguidance
C) The correct view
D) An intermediate position

Key Takeaway
Existence is not necessary; claiming it is necessary is a grave error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live with the constant awareness that your existence is merely possible, or do you act as if it is necessary and self-sustaining? How would your reliance on God change if you truly internalized that you could cease to exist at any moment?
?
When you look at your possessions, achievements, or relationships, do you treat them as if they are necessary to your happiness? In what specific way does this lesson's warning against necessary existence challenge that attachment?
?
The author labels the belief in necessary existence as 'far misguidance'. Is there any belief or assumption in your life that you hold so tightly that it has become 'necessary' in your mind, perhaps leading you away from complete dependence on God? What would it take to release that as merely possible?

Disagreement on Accident vs Substance Existence
Commentary on the accident vs substance debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate on whether existence is an accident, a substance, or neither, and why the author rejects the accident view.

Core Idea
Existence is neither accident nor substance; it is a category above both.

The third disagreement concerns the ontological category of existence. Some say it is an accident, others a substance, and yet others say it is neither. The author affirms that existence is neither accident nor substance, because both are subdivisions of possible existence. Let's examine the author's reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage from 'ومنها اختلافهم في أنه عرض أو جوهر' to the end of the source text.
The passage is cut off, but we have the author's main point.
2
Identify the three positions
The disagreement involves three positions: existence is an accident, existence is a substance, or existence is neither.
The author clearly states the truth is neither.
3
Understand the author's truth
The author says existence is neither accident nor substance because both are divisions of possible existent things. Existence itself is not a possible existent; it is the condition for them.
Accident and substance are categories of beings, not existence itself.
4
Analyze the rejection of accident view
The author notes that Imam al-Haramayn and many theologians considered existence an accident, but he finds it far-fetched because an accident subsists in a subject that is ontologically prior, but existence cannot be dependent on a prior subject.
Accidents require a substance, but existence is not a property of a pre-existing thing.

The Paradox of Existence: Agreed But Disputed
The author summarizes major disagreements among scholars about existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six major philosophical disagreements about the nature of existence (wujūd) among Islamic scholars.

Core Idea
Existence is self-evident, yet its nature is hotly debated.

This passage presents a breathtaking paradox: everyone agrees that existence is the most obvious concept, yet they disagree about almost everything else concerning it. The author invites us to wonder at this state of affairs before listing the key points of dispute that will be explored in the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how the author begins with a question and an expression of wonder.
2
Identify the common ground
The author states that everyone agrees existence is self-evident (badīhī). Reflect on why this is paradoxical given the subsequent disagreements.
3
List the six disputes
Go through each of the six disputes mentioned: particular/universal, necessary/contingent, accident/not accident, existent/mental consideration, identical to essence/superadded, and the word's type (shared/univocal/analogical).
These disputes set the stage for the detailed discussions that follow.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why these disagreements matter for theology and philosophy. Each dispute has implications for how we understand God and creation.
For example, if existence is an accident, then it is separate from essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اختلاف العقلاء في الوجود هو جزئي أو كلي؟ (قال) وما أعجب حال الوجود أطبقوا على أنه بديهي لا أعرف منه، ثم اختلفوا في أنه جزئي أو كلي، واجب أو ممكن عرض أو لا عرض ولا جوهر موجود أو اعتباري لا تحقق له في الأعيان أو واسطة، وأفراده عين الماهيات أو زائدة ولفظه مشترك، أو متواطىء، أو مشكك.
The dispute of the rational people about existence: is it particular or universal? He said: How wondrous is the state of existence! They agree that it is self-evident (badīhī) – nothing is more known than it – then they dispute: is it particular or universal, necessary or contingent, an accident or not an accident and not a substance, existent or an intellectual consideration with no realization in external entities, or an intermediary; and its individuals are identical to essences or superadded; and its word is shared (mushtarak), or univocal (mutawāṭiʾ), or analogical (mushakkik).
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is an overview of the many disagreements about existence (al-wujūd). It begins with a question: Is existence particular or universal? Then the author expresses wonder: everyone agrees existence is the most self-evident thing, yet they differ on its nature. The author then lists the main points of dispute: (1) whether existence is particular or universal, (2) necessary or contingent, (3) an accident, or not an accident and not a substance, (4) an actual existent or a mere mental consideration, (5) whether particular existences are identical to essences or superadded, and (6) whether the word 'existence' is shared, univocal, or analogical. This sets the stage for the detailed discussions that follow.

Is Existence Particular or Universal?
Taftazani explains and resolves the dispute over particular vs. universal existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arguments for and against existence being particular or universal, and the author's resolution in favor of universality.

Core Idea
Existence is a universal concept, not a particular entity.

Theologians have debated whether existence is a single particular reality or a universal applicable to many individuals. Saʿd al-Dīn al-Taftāzānī, the author of Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid, presents this dispute and offers his own resolution. In this passage, he evaluates the claim that existence is a true particular and concludes that it is a universal, with existences as its instances.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Pay attention to the author's initial expression of wonder.
2
Identify the two opposing views
The passage presents two positions: one holds existence is a true particular with no multiplicity; the other (the truth) holds it is a universal with individual existences as its instances.
The phrase 'فقيل' introduces the first view, 'والحق' introduces the author's own.
3
Understand the analogy
The particularist view uses an analogy: saying 'existence of Zayd' is like saying 'god of Zayd'—the term 'existence' is not shared in a common sense but is unique to each individual. Grasp why this analogy supports particularity.
The analogy hinges on the idea that 'god' is not a universal shared by multiple objects.
4
Reflect on the author's resolution
The author declares the truth to be that existence is a universal (kullī) and the various existences are its individuals (afrād). Consider how this resolves the dispute and why it matters for theology.
This conclusion aligns with the common understanding that existence is a concept applicable to many things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يتعجب من اختلافات العقلاء في أحوال الوجود ومع اتفاقهم على أنه أعرف أن الغالب من حال الشيء أن تتبع ذانه في الجلاء والخفاء، فمنها اختلافهم في أنه جزئي أو كلي. فقيل: جزئي حقيقي لا تعدد فيه أصلاً. وإنما في الموجودات بواسطة الإضافات، حتى إن قولنا وجود زيد أو وجود عمرو بمنزلة قولنا: إله زيد وإله عمرو. والحق أنه كلي والوجودات أفراده.
He marvels at the differences of the rational people concerning the states of existence, despite their agreement that it is the best known. For the common case of a thing is that it follows its essence in clarity and obscurity. So among them is their dispute: is it particular or universal? For it was said: it is a true particular with no multiplicity at all, and that multiplicity is only in the existents by way of relations, such that our saying 'existence of Zayd' and 'existence of ʿAmr' is like our saying 'god of Zayd' and 'god of ʿAmr'. And the truth is that it is a universal and the existences are its individuals.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument on the first dispute: whether existence is particular or universal. The author marvels that despite everyone agreeing existence is the most evident thing, they differ in its assessment. The author explains that the usual pattern is that clarity or obscurity follows the essence itself. He then presents the view that existence is a true particular (juzʾī ḥaqīqī) with no multiplicity in itself; multiplicity only occurs in existents through relations (iḍāfāt). For example, saying 'existence of Zayd' is analogous to saying 'god of Zayd' – the term 'existence' is not shared in a common sense. But the author declares the truth (al-ḥaqq) to be that existence is a universal (kullī) and the individual existences are its instances (afrād). Thus he resolves the dispute in favor of universality.

Key Terms
جزئي
كلي
إضافات
أفراد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because we speak of 'existence of Zayd' and 'existence of Amr', existence is multiple and particular.
Correction: The author clarifies it is a universal concept; the phrase 'existence of' does not make it particular.

Checkpoint
What is the author's final position on whether existence is particular or universal?
A) Existence is a true particular with no multiplicity
B) Existence is a universal and existences are its individuals
C) Existence is neither particular nor universal
D) Existence is particular due to relations

Key Takeaway
Existence is a universal concept whose instances are individual existences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own existence, do you tend to think of it as uniquely yours or as a share in a universal reality? How does that perspective change the way you value others' existence?
?
If existence is a universal concept, then your existence is not ultimately separate from others'. How does that challenge your feelings of pride or isolation? What would it mean to see every person as a fellow instantiation of the same gift?
?
The passage says existence is the most evident thing, yet we differ about its nature. Are you aware that you are taking your existence for granted even now? What would it take to live today with a constant awareness that you exist only by the One whose existence is necessary?

Necessary vs. Contingent Existence
The author rejects the view that existence is necessary, calling it far error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Islamic theologians (kalam) affirm that existence as such is not necessary; only God's existence is necessary, and any claim otherwise is considered far error.

Core Idea
Only God's existence is necessary; all else is contingent.

In this passage, the author addresses a controversial theological claim: that existence itself is necessary. He reports that many later scholars held this view, but he strongly rejects it as 'far error.' This lesson clarifies why that position is unacceptable within Sunni kalam and what the correct stance is.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English to grasp the author's strong rejection.
Note the phrase 'al-ḍalāl al-baʿīd'.
2
Understand the dispute
Recognize that the debate is whether existence is wājib (necessary) or mumkin (contingent).
3
Focus on the author's judgment
Why is calling existence necessary considered 'far error'? Relate to the uniqueness of God's necessity.
Only God's existence is wājib.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها اختلافهم في أنه واجب أو ممكن. فقد ذهب جمع كثير من المتأخرين إلى أنه واجب على ما ذكرنا، وذلك هو الضلال البعيد.
And among them is their dispute: is it necessary or contingent? For a great many of the latecomers have held that it is necessary, as we mentioned, and that is far error (al-ḍalāl al-baʿīd).
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage presents the second dispute: whether existence is necessary (wājib) or contingent (mumkin). The author reports that many later scholars (mutaʾakhirūn) have taken the position that existence itself is necessary. However, the author strongly rejects this, calling it 'far error' (al-ḍalāl al-baʿīd). The phrase 'as we mentioned' likely refers to an earlier discussion where the opinion was explained. The author does not provide further detail here, but the judgment is clear: existence is not necessary; it is contingent or perhaps predicated differently. This warning sets the stage for the correct doctrine that only God's existence is necessary.

Existence: Neither Substance nor Accident
The author refutes the view that existence is an accident by appealing to the definition of accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why classical theologians debated whether existence is a substance or an accident, and why the correct view is that existence is neither, based on the definition of an accident.

Core Idea
Existence is not an accident because nothing can be prior to existence.

Theologians have debated whether existence is an accident, a substance, or neither. This passage clarifies the correct position: existence is more fundamental than either category. We'll examine the reasoning behind this conclusion and why other views are problematic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Note the three positions mentioned at the beginning.
The passage is dense; take your time to absorb each phrase.
2
Identify the three positions
The passage presents three views on existence: it is a substance, it is an accident, or it is neither. Which one does the author affirm?
The author says 'this is the truth' (wa-hādhā huwa al-haqq) for the third view.
3
Understand the definition of accident
The author defines an accident as something that is not self-subsisting but requires a locus that is independent of it in priority. Ask yourself: can existence fit this definition?
The key term is 'taqaddum' (priority).
4
Grasp the deeper principle
The author argues that existence cannot be preceded by anything—it is the most fundamental reality. Therefore, it cannot be an accident. Reflect on why this is a decisive refutation.
Think about the ontological priority of existence over all other categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها اختلافهم في أنه عرض أو جوهر، أو ليس بعرض ولا جوهر، لكونهما من أقسام الممكن الموجود وهذا هو الحق. وفي كلام الإمام ما يشعر أنه عرض وبه صرح جمع كثير من المتكلمين، وهو بعيد جداً لأن العرض ما لا يتقوم بنفسه بل بمحله المستغني عنه في تقدمه، ولا يتصور في تقدمه وتحققه عن الوجود.
And among them is their dispute: is it an accident or a substance, or neither an accident nor a substance, because they are divisions of contingent existent, and this is the truth. In the words of the Imam [al-Ashʿarī?] there is something suggesting that it is an accident, and many theologians have explicitly stated it, but it is very far-fetched because an accident is what is not self-subsisting but rather subsists in a locus that is independent of it in its priority, and it is inconceivable that it be prior to or realized without existence.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage addresses the third dispute: whether existence is an accident (ʿaraḍ), a substance (jawhar), or neither. The author affirms the correct view is that existence is neither substance nor accident, because both are categories of contingent existents, whereas existence itself is not a contingent existent in that sense. The author then mentions that some statements of the Imam (likely al-Ashʿarī) might suggest existence is an accident, and many theologians explicitly said so. But the author rejects this as very far-fetched (baʿīd jiddan). The reason: an accident is defined as something that does not subsist by itself but requires a locus (maḥall) that is independent of it in priority (taqaddum). Existence cannot be dependent on something else in priority because everything else depends on existence. Therefore, existence cannot be an accident. This argument is based on the principle that existence is more fundamental than any subject.

Key Terms

جوهر
محل
تقدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think existence is an accident because it is predicated of substances.
Correction: Accidents depend on a subject that is prior to them, but existence is more fundamental than any subject; nothing is prior to existence.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the view that existence is an accident?
A) Because accident is a category of contingent things
B) Because an accident depends on a subject that is prior to it, but nothing is prior to existence
C) Because the Imam said it is a substance
D) Because existence is a substance

Key Takeaway
Existence is more fundamental than any subject, so it cannot be an accident that inheres in a subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very existence is not an accident but the foundation of everything, why do you often act as if your existence is something you control or deserve? How can you shift to seeing every moment you exist as a direct gift from Allah, not a given right?
?
The source argues that existence cannot depend on anything else—it is prior. When you feel your worth depends on your achievements, status, or others' opinions, you are treating your existence as if it were an accident. What would it mean for you to truly anchor your identity in the sheer fact that Allah has given you existence, regardless of anything else?
?
Since existence is more fundamental than any subject, including yourself, who are you really? If you strip away all accidents—your body, your mind, your possessions—what remains? That is your existence, a reality sustained by Allah alone. Are you prepared to stand before Him with nothing but that?

The Two Positions on the Existence of Existence
Dispute over the existence of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main positions on whether existence itself is an existent, and the initial argument that leads to a dilemma.

Core Idea
The debate: is existence an existent by itself or a mere mental concept?

In this lesson, we encounter the opening of the fourth dispute concerning existence. Some claim existence is an existent entity identical with itself, while others argue it is a pure mental consideration. The author presents these views and the immediate logical problem: if existence existed, it would either require an additional existence or cause a semantic inconsistency. We will explore this foundational question that sets up deeper arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read the first part of the passage to find the two opposite claims regarding whether existence itself is existent.
The first position avoids regress by making existence identical with its own existence.
2
Understand the dilemma
Notice the conditional argument: if existence existed, it would either have an additional existence (regress) or be identical (causing univocity problems).
This dilemma is the starting point for further arguments.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this question matters: it concerns the nature of existence itself, which underpins all metaphysical discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها اختلافهم في أنه موجود أو لا فقيل: موجود بوجود هو نفسه فلا يتسلسل. وقيل: بل اعتباري محض لا تحقق له في الأعيان إذ لو وجد فإما أن يوجد بوجود زائد فيتسلسل، أو بوجود هو نفسه فلا يكون إطلاق الموجود على الوجود وعلى سائر الأشياء بمعنى واحد، لأن معناه في الوجود أنه الوجود، وفي غيره أنه ذو الوجود، ولأنه إما أن يكون جوهراً فلا يقع صفة للأشياء، أو عرضاً فيتقوم المحل دونه والتقوم بدون الوجود محال، ولأن ما ذكر في زيادة الوجود على الماهية من أنا نعقل الماهية ونشك في وجودها جار بعينه في وجود الوجود فإنا نعقل الوجود ونشك في وجوده فلو وجد لكان وجوده زائداً وتسلسل. وبهذا يتبين بطلان ما ذهب إليه الفلاسفة من أن ماهية الواجب نفس الوجود المجرد. وذلك لأنا بعد ما نتصور الوجود المجرد نطلب بالبرهان وجوده في الأعيان، فيكون وجوده زائداً ويتسلسل ولا محيص إلا بأن الوجود المقول على الوجودات اعتبار عقلي كما سبق. وقيل: الوجود ليس بموجود ولا معدوم، بل واسطة على ما سيأتي.
And among them is their dispute: is it existent or not? For it was said: it exists by an existence that is itself, so there is no infinite regress. And it was said: rather it is a pure mental consideration with no realization in external entities, because if it existed, then either it would exist by an additional existence and thus lead to infinite regress, or by an existence that is itself, and then the application of 'existent' to existence and to other things would not be in a single sense, because its meaning in existence is that it is existence, and in other things that they have existence; and because it would either be a substance and then not occur as a description of things, or an accident and then the subject would subsist without it, but subsistence without existence is impossible; and because what was mentioned regarding the addition of existence to essence—that we conceive the essence and doubt its existence—applies exactly to the existence of existence: we conceive existence and doubt its existence; so if it existed, its existence would be additional and lead to infinite regress. And by this the falsity of what the philosophers held—that the essence of the Necessary is pure existence—becomes clear. That is because after we conceive pure existence, we seek by proof its existence in external entities, so its existence would be additional and regress, and there is no escape except that existence predicated of existences is a mental consideration (iʿtibār ʿaqlī), as previously mentioned. And it was said: existence is neither existent nor non-existent, but an intermediary, as will come.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument on the fourth dispute: whether existence (al-wujūd) itself is an existent entity (mawjūd) or not. Two main positions are presented. First, some say it is existent by an existence that is itself (mawjūd bi-wujūd huwa nafsuhu), avoiding infinite regress. Second, others say it is a mere mental consideration (iʿtibārī maḥḍ) with no external realization. The author elaborates the second view with multiple arguments: if existence were an existent, either its existence would be additional (zāʾid) leading to regress, or it would be identical, but then the term 'existent' would not be univocal; also, existence cannot be a substance (because then it wouldn't predicate of others) or an accident (because the subject would need existence to subsist). Furthermore, the same argument used for the addition of existence to essence applies to existence itself: we can conceive existence and doubt its existence, so if it existed, its existence would be additional, leading to regress. The author then uses this to refute the philosophers' claim that the essence of the Necessary (God) is pure existence: because pure existence as conceived requires proof for its external existence, leading to regress unless existence is merely a mental consideration. Finally, a third view is mentioned: existence is neither existent nor non-existent but an intermediary (wāsiṭa), to be explained later.

Key Terms
موجود
اعتباري
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Existence is an existent entity like any other.
Correction: If existence were an existent, it would require an additional existence or cause terminological inconsistency, leading to absurdity.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if existence existed by an existence that is itself, according to the passage?
A) It would lead to infinite regress.
B) The term 'existent' would not apply in a single sense to existence and other things.
C) Existence would be a substance.
D) Existence would be a mental consideration.

Key Takeaway
The initial dilemma shows that existence as an existent leads to either regress or semantic inconsistency, setting up the need for a deeper analysis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If existence itself is merely a mental concept, then what is the foundation of your certainty that anything at all exists? Does your faith rest on a conceptual structure or on direct encounter? The argument in this passage strips away any easy assumption—are you willing to examine the basis of your conviction even if it feels unsettling?
?
When you recite 'There is no god but Allah,' you affirm His existence. But if the meaning of 'existence' is itself problematic, what exactly are you affirming? Does this passage challenge you to redefine what you mean by God's reality beyond a mere mental label? What would change in your worship if God's existence is not a property but something altogether beyond?
?
The second position avoids infinite regress by denying that existence is an entity. Consider a personal belief or habit that rests on a chain of justifications. Can you trace it back to a foundation that does not itself require further justification? Are you comfortable with a foundation that might be merely conceptual? This lesson forces you to face the specter of regress in your own life—will you stop the chain or keep adding links?
