Arguments Against Existence as an Existent and Refutation of Philosophers
Arguments proving existence is a mental consideration and refutation of the philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed arguments against existence being an actual existent, and how they refute the philosophers' claim that God's essence is pure existence.

Core Idea
Existence cannot be an existent without leading to infinite regress, so it is a mental consideration.

Having seen the initial dilemma, the author now presents further arguments that demonstrate the impossibility of existence being an actual entity. He argues that existence cannot be a substance or an accident, and uses the same reasoning about essence and existence to show that existence itself would require an additional existence. These arguments culminate in a refutation of the philosophers who identify God's essence with pure existence, and a third view is mentioned as a resolution.

The Term 'Existence': Shared, Univocal, or Analogical
The author enumerates three positions on the term 'existence' and declares the analogical view as true.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three views on the word 'existence' and why the author affirms it as analogical (mushakkik).

Core Idea
The word 'existence' is used with one meaning but not equally—it is analogical.

This lesson examines a key linguistic dispute in kalam: how we apply the word 'existence' to things. Does it mean something different for God and creatures? The passage presents three positions and settles on the view that existence is analogical (mushakkik). This nuance is crucial for understanding how kalam talks about divine attributes and created things.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the three positions are introduced with 'or' (aw).
2
Identify the three positions
Identify each view: mushtarak (shared), mutawāṭiʾ (univocal), mushakkik (analogical). Note how each is defined.
Mushtarak has multiple meanings; mutawāṭiʾ has one equal meaning; mushakkik has one meaning but not equally.
3
Understand why analogical is correct
The author declares mushakkik as the truth. Reflect on why this view is important: it allows existence to be predicated of God and creatures with a shared concept but with priority and intensity.
This view balances unity and difference in attributing existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها اختلافهم في أن لفظ الوجود مشترك بين مفهومات مختلفة على ما نقل عن الأشعري، أو متواطىء يقع على الوجودات بمعنى واحد لا تفاوت فيه أصلاً، أو مشكك يقع عليها بمعنى واحد هو مفهوم الكون لكن لا على السواء. وهو الحق.
And among them is their dispute: whether the word 'existence' is shared (mushtarak) among different concepts, as transmitted from al-Ashʿarī, or univocal (mutawāṭiʾ) applying to existences with one meaning no difference at all, or analogical (mushakkik) applying to them with one meaning – the concept of being (kawn) – but not equally. And it is the truth.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth and final dispute: how the word 'existence' (lafẓ al-wujūd) is used. Three positions are given: (1) It is shared (mushtarak) among different concepts – meaning the term has multiple unrelated meanings, as reported from al-Ashʿarī. (2) It is univocal (mutawāṭiʾ) – applied equally to all existences with exactly the same meaning. (3) It is analogical (mushakkik) – applied with one common meaning (the concept of being, kawn) but not equally, i.e., with priority and posteriority or intensity. The author declares that the correct view is the third: existence is an analogical term (mushakkik). This is the truth (wa huwa al-ḥaqq). This classification is important in kalam because it allows existence to be predicated of God and creatures in different ways while maintaining a conceptual unity.

Key Terms
مشترك
متواطىء
مشكك
مفهوم الكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Existence means the same for all beings, so God and creatures exist in exactly the same way.
Correction: Existence is analogical: it has one core meaning but is applied to God and creatures with priority and intensity, so they differ in how they possess existence.

Checkpoint
What is the author's final position on the word 'existence'?
A) It is purely shared (mushtarak)
B) It is univocal (mutawāṭiʾ)
C) It is analogical (mushakkik)
D) It is none of the above

Key Takeaway
The correct view is that existence is analogical (mushakkik): it applies to all existents with one core concept (being) but not equally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Before reading this passage, did you assume that God 'exists' in exactly the same way you exist? Now that you know the correct view is analogical, how would you complete this sentence: 'When I say God exists, I mean _________.' Write down one concrete change this truth requires in your daily awareness of God.
?
If existence is one concept but not equally shared, then your existence is entirely dependent and contingent. Are you truly comfortable with being contingent before the Necessary Existent? What would it look like for you to live today with that profound dependency at the forefront of your mind?
?
The author declares the analogical view 'the truth' (وهو الحق). Have you ever personally invested effort to settle a truth about God, or do you take the first comfortable understanding that comes? Before this lesson ends, commit to one act that shows you are seeking the truth about His existence—not just academic, but lived.

The Three Indications: Rational and Conventional
The author enumerates three indications and classifies them as rational or conventional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hierarchy of existence and the three types of indication, distinguishing the purely rational indication from the two conventional ones.

Core Idea
Some indications are fixed by reason; others vary by convention.

The author explains a crucial framework for understanding how knowledge is transmitted through different levels of existence. Starting from external objects, we move to mental forms, then to spoken words, and finally to written script. Each level points back to the previous one, creating three distinct types of indication. This passage clarifies which of these indications are universal and which are subject to human convention.

Guided Path
1
Understand the hierarchy of existence
Note the order: external existence → mental existence → verbal existence → written existence. Each subsequent level indicates the previous.
This hierarchy is the foundation for the three indications.
2
Identify the three indications
The mental indicates the external (first), the verbal indicates the mental (second), and the written indicates the verbal (third).
3
Distinguish rational from conventional
The first indication (mental→external) is purely rational and does not vary. The other two are conventional and allow variation.
Rational indication is universal; conventional depends on human agreement.
4
Examine variation in conventional indications
In the second indication (verbal→mental), only the indicator (word) changes. In the third (written→verbal), both indicator (script) and indicated (utterance) can change.
Example: 'al-sama' in Arabic vs. Persian, and different scripts for the same word.
5
Reflect on the example
Consider the word 'al-sama' as used in the passage. It illustrates both types of conventional variation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولكل لاحق فيما ذكرنا من الترتيب دلالة على السابق فللذهني على العيني، وللفظي على الذهني، وللخطي على اللفظي، فتحقق ثلاث دلالات أولاها: عقلية محضة : لا يختلف فيها بحسب اختلاف الأشخاص والأوضاع الدال ولا المدلول، إذا بأي لفظ عبر عن السماء . فالموجود منها في الخارج هو الشخص وفي الذهن هو الصورة المعينة المطابقة له ، والأخريان أعني دلالة اللفظ على الصورة الذهنية ودلالة الخط على اللفظ وضعيتان يختلف في الأولى منهما الدال بأن تعين طائفة لفظاً (۲) كالسماء ، وطائفة أخرى لفظاً آخر كما في الفارسية وغيرها لا المدلول لأن الصورة الذهنية لا تختلف باختلاف اللغات وتختلف في الثانية: أعني دلالة الخط على اللفظ الدال والمدلول جميعاً، واختلاف الدال لا يختص بحالة اختلاف المدلول، بل قد يكون (۳) مع كلفظ السماء، يكتب بصور مختلفة بحسب اختلاف الاصطلاحات في الكتابة .
And each subsequent one, in the order we have mentioned, indicates the previous: the mental indicates the external, the verbal indicates the mental, and the written indicates the verbal. Thus three indications come about. The first of them is purely rational: it does not differ, according to difference of persons and conventions, in either the indicator or the indicated, for whatever word one expresses 'the sky' by, what exists in the external is the individual and in the mind is the specific form corresponding to it. The other two, namely the indication of the utterance to the mental form and the indication of writing to the utterance, are conventional. In the first of them, the indicator varies, in that a group assigns a word such as 'al-sama'' and another group assigns another word as in Persian and others, not the indicated, because the mental form does not differ with difference of languages. And in the second, namely the indication of writing to the utterance, both the indicator and the indicated vary. And the variation of the indicator is not restricted to a case of variation of the indicated; rather it may occur, as with the word 'al-sama'', which is written in different forms according to different conventions in writing.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage describes the three indications that arise from the hierarchy of existence. The author states that each subsequent level indicates the previous one: mental existence indicates external existence, verbal existence indicates mental existence, and written existence indicates verbal existence. Thus three types of indication emerge. The first indication (mental to external) is purely rational (عقلية محضة): it does not vary with different persons or conventions; for example, whatever word one uses for 'the sky' in speech, what exists externally is the individual sky, and what exists mentally is the specific form of it. This indication is universal and invariant. The other two indications are conventional (وضعيتان). In the first of these (verbal indication of the mental form), only the indicator (the word) varies, as different languages use different words for the same concept (e.g., 'al-sama'' in Arabic, 'āseman' in Persian), but the indicated (the mental form) does not change because the mental image is not language-dependent. In the second (written indication of the utterance), both the indicator (the script) and the indicated (the utterance) can vary, since the same word may be written in different scripts according to different writing conventions. Thus the author distinguishes the degrees of variability: rational indication is fixed, while conventional indications allow change in the indicator or both.

Key Terms
دلالة عقلية
دلالة وضعية
دال
مدلول
لفظ
خط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All indications are conventional and can be changed at will.
Correction: The first indication (mental to external) is purely rational and invariant; it does not depend on human convention.

Checkpoint
In the second conventional indication (writing indicating utterance), which of the following can vary?
A) Only the indicator
B) Only the indicated
C) Both the indicator and the indicated
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The first indication (mental to external) is purely rational and invariant, while the other two (verbal to mental and written to verbal) are conventional and subject to variation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says the mental form of 'sky' is universal across languages. What about concepts like 'justice' or 'mercy'? Do you believe they are universal too, or do they vary? How does your answer affect how you treat others who differ from you in these matters?
?
You rely on written texts like the Quran. The passage teaches that writing is a conventional indication where both script and utterance can vary. How do you rationally ascertain that the Qur'an you read today is the same as what was revealed? What else besides convention do you rely on?
?
If the mental-to-external indication is purely rational and unchanging, then some truths about reality are fixed. Which truths in your life have you accepted merely by convention (e.g., from your culture or family) that might actually be rationally fixed? Are you willing to reconsider them?

The Three Premises of the Proof for Mental Existence
Al-Sanusi presents three premises for the reality of mental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three premises that al-Sanusi uses to prove that concepts have a mental existence.

Core Idea
We can judge about non-existents; therefore they must exist in the mind.

Al-Sanusi begins his proof for mental existence by pointing to three undeniable facts about our thinking: we make judgments about impossible things, we conceive of universals, and we entertain propositions that range beyond actual existents. Each of these shows that the mind contains objects that have no external counterpart. Let's examine each premise.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that each premise is introduced with 'بأن'.
2
Identify the three types of mental content
Find the three distinct premises: judgments on impossibilities, universal concepts, and true propositions beyond existents.
Think about why each requires mental existence.
3
Ask yourself why each requires mental existence
For each premise, consider: if the subject doesn't exist externally, where does it exist? The proof forces an answer: in the mind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستدلال على إثبات الوجود الذهني. قال: ويستدل على تحقق الذهني: بأن نحكم إيجاباً على ما لا ثبوت له في الخارج، كالممتنعات مع استحالة الإثبات لما لا ثبوت له، وبأن نجد من المفهومات ما هو كلي يمتنع بكليته في الخارج. ومن القضايا حقيقية لا يقتصر الحكم فيها على الموجود في الخارج، واعترض بأنه يكفي في الإيجاب تميز الموضوع عند العقل، وهو معنى التعقل فيرجع الكل إلى أن الفهم والتعقل، يقتضي الثبوت في العقل وفيه النزاع. والجواب: أن اقتضاء التعقل والتميز إضافة بين العاقل والمعقول ضروري ولا تعقل الإضافة إلى النفي السرف، بل لا بد من ثبوت ما وإذ ليس في الخارج ففي العقل. فإن قيل: يجوز أن يقوم بنفسه، كالمثل المجردة لأفلاطون والمعلقة لغيره، أو ببعض المجردات كصور الكائنات بالعقل الفعال عند الفلاسفة. قلنا: معلوم بالضرورة أن الممتنع بل المعدوم سيما ما ليس من قبيل الذوات لا يقوم بنفسه، ولا ببعض المجردات بهويته بل بصورته، وفيه المدعي.
The proof for establishing mental existence. He said: And one infers the reality of the mental (al-dhihnī) by our making an affirmative judgment about that which has no subsistence in the external, like impossibilities, despite the impossibility of affirming what has no subsistence; and by our finding among concepts that which is universal, the entirety of which is impossible in the external; and from propositions that are true (ḥaqīqiyya) whose judgment is not restricted to what exists in the external. And it is objected that in affirmative judgment, the distinction of the subject in the intellect suffices, and that is the meaning of intellection, so everything reduces to the fact that understanding and intellection necessitate subsistence in the intellect, and therein lies the dispute. The answer: The necessity of intellection and distinction is a relation between the intellect and the intelligible, and a relation to pure negation is inconceivable; rather, there must be some subsistence, and since it is not in the external, it is in the intellect. If it is said: It is possible that it subsists by itself, like the Platonic Forms or the suspended forms for others, or by some abstract entities like the forms of beings through the Active Intellect according to the philosophers. We said: It is known necessarily that the impossible, even the non-existent, especially what is not of the category of essences, does not subsist by itself nor by some abstract entities in its identity, but rather by its image, and therein is the claim.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage presents a formal proof (istidlāl) for the existence of mental existence (al-wujūd al-dhihnī). The proof is based on three premises: (1) We make affirmative judgments about things that have no external existence, such as impossibilities (e.g., 'the conjunction of opposites is impossible'), despite the fact that affirming something that has no subsistence is impossible in the external. (2) We find concepts that are universal, and a universal as a whole cannot exist externally. (3) We have 'true propositions' (qaḍāyā ḥaqīqiyya) whose judgments are not limited to externally existing individuals. After stating the proof, an objection is raised: in an affirmative judgment, the mere distinction (tamyīz) of the subject in the mind suffices, and that is the essence of intellection; so the whole argument just shows that understanding requires subsistence in the mind, which is exactly what is disputed. The author's answer: Intellection and distinction necessitate a relation between the intellect and the intelligible, and a relation to pure negation is unintelligible; there must be something that subsists, and if not externally, then in the intellect. A further objection: the subject could subsist by itself (like Platonic Forms) or through abstract entities (like the Active Intellect). The author responds: It is self-evident that the impossible, or even the non-existent (especially that which is not a substance), does not subsist by itself or by abstract entities in its own identity, but only by its image (ṣūra), which is precisely the claim of mental existence.

Key Terms
الوجود الذهني
الممتنعات
القضايا الحقيقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think we can think about nothing without any mental content.
Correction: The proof shows that even thinking about impossibilities requires a mental object that is the subject of judgment.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the premises used to prove mental existence?
A) Judgments about impossibilities
B) The existence of universal concepts
C) The existence of true propositions about the non-existent
D) The fact that we can imagine impossible objects

Key Takeaway
Mental existence is proven by the need to have subjects for judgments about non-existents, universals, and unrestricted propositions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a judgment about something that does not exist in the external world—like your own future intentions—do you realize that this judgment has both a mental reality and a moral weight? How will this awareness change the way you deliberate?
?
You habitually think about universals, like 'every human is mortal,' but do you apply the truth of that judgment to yourself? Are you living as if the universal applies to you specifically?
?
The proof shows that mental existence is necessary for intelligible thought. Have you ever considered that your thoughts about God—even incorrect ones—have a mental reality that either aligns with or contradicts divine truth? Does this make you more careful about what you entertain in your mind?

Defending Mental Existence: Objections and Responses
Al-Sanusi refutes two objections against mental existence: the sufficiency of mental distinction and the possibility of external subsistence through separate forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections raised against the proof for mental existence and how al-Sanusi rebuts them, including the argument from the relation of intellection and the alternative of separate forms.

Core Idea
Intellection requires a relation, and relation requires a real object — so mental existence is necessary.

After presenting the proof, al-Sanusi anticipates objections. An opponent might argue that simply distinguishing the subject in the mind is enough, without additional mental existence. Al-Sanusi responds by analyzing the nature of intellection as a relation. Another objection appeals to Platonic Forms or the Active Intellect. Let's see how he defends mental existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response carefully
Read the Arabic passage that contains the objection, the answer, the second objection, and the final response. Understand the flow.
Notice the structure: واعترض, والجواب, فإن قيل, قلنا.
2
Understand the relation argument
The key point: intellection is a relation. A relation requires two relata. If the object has no existence externally, it must exist mentally.
Consider why a relation to pure negation is impossible.
3
Consider the alternative of separate forms and why it fails
Some propose that the object subsists by itself (Platonic Forms) or through the Active Intellect. Al-Sanusi argues that the impossible cannot subsist in itself; it only exists as an image in the mind.
This directly supports mental existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The proof for establishing mental existence. He said: And one infers the reality of the mental (al-dhihnī) by our making an affirmative judgment about that which has no subsistence in the external, like impossibilities, despite the impossibility of affirming what has no subsistence; and by our finding among concepts that which is universal, the entirety of which is impossible in the external; and from propositions that are true (ḥaqīqiyya) whose judgment is not restricted to what exists in the external. The proof is based on three premises: (1) We make affirmative judgments about things that have no external existence, such as impossibilities (e.g., 'the conjunction of opposites is impossible'), despite the fact that affirming something that has no subsistence is impossible in the external. ?

The First Proof and the Objection of Sufficient Distinction
First proof for mental existence and the initial objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof for mental existence and the initial objection that mental distinction alone is sufficient for affirmative judgments.

Core Idea
We cannot affirm something of pure nothingness, so impossibilities must have mental existence.

Sanusi begins by laying out what he calls the first proof: we make affirmative judgments about things that have no external existence, such as the impossible. Since it is impossible to affirm something of a pure non-existent, these impossibilities must have some kind of existence — and that is mental existence. He then anticipates an objection: perhaps all that is needed is mental distinction, not a separate mode of existence. This sets up the debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning through the proof of mental existence using affirmative judgments, universal concepts, and real propositions.
Notice how the proof uses three separate pieces of evidence.
2
Identify the objection
Find where the objector says that mental distinction (tamyīz) is sufficient for affirmation, which would undermine the need for mental existence.
The objection is introduced by 'wa‘turiḍa bi-annahu yakfī...'
3
See the author's initial response
Read the author's answer that relation to pure negation is impossible, so there must be some existent term, if not externally then in the mind.
This response is the start of the defense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: ويستدل على تحقق الذهني (١) : بأن نحكم إيجاباً على ما لا ثبوت له ، في الخارج، كالممتنعات (۲) مع استحالة الإثبات لما لا ثبوت له، وبأن نجد من المفهومات ما هو كلي يمتنع بكليته في الخارج. ومن القضايا حقيقية لا يقتصر الحكم فيها على الموجود في الخارج، واعترض بأنه يكفي في الإيجاب تميز الموضوع عند العقل، وهو معنى التعقل فيرجع الكل إلى أن الفهم والتعقل، يقتضي الثبوت في العقل وفيه النزاع . والجواب : أن اقتضاء التعقل والتميز إضافة بين العاقل والمعقول ضروري ولا تعقل الإضافة إلى النفي السرف، بل لا بد من ثبوت (ما) وإذ ليس في الخارج ففي العقل . فإن قيل : يجوز أن يقوم بنفسه ، كالمثل المجردة (۳) لأفلاطون والمعلقة لغيره، أو ببعض المجردات كصور الكائنات بالعقل الفعال عند الفلاسفة قلنا : معلوم بالضرورة أن الممتنع (٤) بل المعدوم سيما (٥) ما ليس من قبيل الذوات لا يقوم بنفسه، ولا ببعض المجردات بهويته بل بصورته، وفيه المدعي
مقابلة
من جهة استلزامه كون التعقل بحصول الصورة لا من جهة استلزامه أن للمعقولات نوعاً من التمييز غير التميز بالهوية الخارجية سواء اخترعه العقل أو لاحظه (۱) من محل آخر، لأن اقتضاء التميز الثبوت في العقل أول المسألة)) . كون العلم سيما العلم بما لا تحقق له في الأعيان مقتضياً لثبوت أمر في الذهن ظاهر يجري مجرى الضروريات، فمن هنا زعم بعضهم أن إنكار الوجود الذهني إنكار للأمر الضروري
He said: Evidence is adduced for the realization of mental existence (al-dhihnī): by our judging affirmatively of that which has no existence in the external world, like impossible things (al-mumtani‘āt), [this] together with the impossibility of affirming (ithbāt) that which has no existence; and by our finding among concepts that which is universal, the universality of which is impossible in the external world; and [by] real propositions (al-qaḍāyā al-ḥaqīqiyya) in which judgment is not restricted to what exists externally. And it is objected that for affirmative judgment it is sufficient that the subject be distinguished in the mind, which is the meaning of intellection (ta‘aqqul); thus the whole reduces to the fact that understanding and intellection necessitate existence in the mind, and therein lies the dispute. The answer: that the requirement of intellection and distinction is a relation between the intellect (‘āqil) and the intelligible (ma‘qūl) necessarily, and relation to pure negation is inconceivable; rather there must be some (mā) existence, and if it is not in the external world, then it is in the mind. For if it is said: it is possible for it to subsist by itself, like the separate Forms (al-muthul al-mujarrada) of Plato and the suspended entities of others, or by some separate intellects like the forms of beings by the Active Intellect according to the philosophers, we say: it is known necessarily that the impossible, nay, the non-existent, especially that which is not among the essences (dhawāt), does not subsist by itself nor by some separate intellects in its very identity (bi-huwiyyatihi) but by its form (ṣūratih), and therein lies the claim. Counterpoint: from the perspective of its implying that intellection occurs by the attainment of the form, not from the perspective of its implying that the intelligibles have a type of distinction other than distinction by external identity, whether the intellect invents it or observes it from another locus, because the requirement of distinction is existence in the mind, which is the beginning of the issue. Knowledge, especially knowledge of that which has no realization in external entities, necessitates the existence of something in the mind; this is apparent and runs in the course of self-evident truths. Hence some have claimed that the denial of mental existence is a denial of a self-evident truth.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage is a proof for mental existence (al-wujūd al-dhihnī). The author begins by presenting the first argument: we make affirmative judgments (aḥkām ījābiyya) about things that have no external existence, such as impossibilities (al-mumtani‘āt). Since affirmative judgment means affirming something of something else, and it is impossible to affirm something of that which has no existence, these impossibilities must have some mode of existence, which is mental existence. Additionally, we have universal concepts that cannot exist universally in the external world, and we have 'real propositions' (al-qaḍāyā al-ḥaqīqiyya) that apply to all individuals of a subject, including those not present externally. This also points to mental existence. The author then presents an objection: for an affirmative judgment, it is sufficient that the subject be mentally distinguished, which is the same as intellection (ta‘aqqul). If so, then the whole argument reduces to the claim that understanding requires existence in the mind, which is exactly what is being disputed. The author answers by explaining that intellection and mental distinction constitute a relation between the intellect and the intelligible. A relation cannot be to pure nothingness; there must be some existent term. If not in the external world, then it must be in the mind. Another objection is raised: perhaps the impossibilities have a separate self-subsistent existence, like Plato's Forms or the suspended entities, or they subsist in the Active Intellect. The author refutes this by stating that it is known necessarily that the non-existent, especially that which is not a distinct essence (dhāt), does not subsist by itself or by another intellect in its very identity (huwiyya), but only as a form (ṣūra) in a mind. This leads to the claim that intellection requires the attainment of the form, not the existence of the object in its external identity. The author further clarifies that the requirement of distinction is exactly what is at issue. Finally, he states that knowledge of non-existents clearly necessitates mental existence, to the point that some consider its denial a denial of a self-evident truth.

Key Terms
وجود ذهني
ممتنع
تعقل
تميز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mental existence is just a conceptual fiction; it has no real basis.
Correction: The author shows that without mental existence, we could not make true affirmative judgments about impossibilities or have universal concepts.

Checkpoint
What is the first evidence given for mental existence?
A) Revelation
B) Affirmative judgments about things without external existence
C) The consensus of philosophers
D) The existence of the soul

Key Takeaway
The first proof for mental existence shows that meaningful affirmative judgments about non-existents require the subjects to have some mode of existence — mental existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You make judgments every day about things you've never seen — mathematical truths, abstract ideas, even fictional characters. According to this passage, these judgments require those objects to have some form of existence in your mind. When you last argued about the concept of 'justice' or 'infinity', did you treat them as real? How does this lesson challenge you to acknowledge the mental reality of such objects and humble yourself before the vastness of what exists beyond the physical?
?
The objector in this passage thought mental distinction was enough without any special mode of existence. Where in your own life do you settle for a vague understanding — a mere 'mental distinction' — instead of truly engaging with the reality of what you think about? When have you said 'I know that' without really having the concept rooted in your mind with clarity and conviction? Commit today to a topic you've merely distinguished, and resolve to learn it deeply so it becomes truly existent in your mind.
?
If relation to pure nothingness is impossible, then every thought you have is a witness to some kind of existence. Consider your most persistent worry or doubt. It exists in your mind — what kind of existence is that? Is it a mere phantom, or does it have a reality that you must address? This lesson teaches that even negative judgments require a subject. What does it mean for you that your fears and hopes are mentally real, and how should that change the way you deal with them from this moment forward?

Refuting the Theory of Separate Subsistence
Refutation of alternative theories and conclusion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's refutation of alternative theories that posit separate existence for non-existents, and the conclusion that mental existence is self-evident.

Core Idea
Non-existents do not subsist by themselves or in separate intellects, but only as forms in a mind.

Having answered the initial objection, the author now faces a more radical challenge: perhaps impossibilities have a separate, self-subsistent existence, like Plato's Forms, or exist in the Active Intellect. He refutes this by arguing that non-existents, especially those that are not essences, cannot subsist on their own or in another intellect in their very identity, but only as forms. He then reinforces his position by stating that the requirement of distinction is the crux of the matter, and that knowledge of non-existents clearly necessitates mental existence, to the point that denying it is like denying a self-evident truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection
Read the passage starting from 'fa-in qīla' where the objector suggests that impossibilities could subsist by themselves like Plato's Forms or in the Active Intellect.
Notice the references to Platonic and Peripatetic theories.
2
Understand the refutation
See how the author argues that non-existents do not subsist by themselves or in separate intellects in their identity, but only as forms.
The refutation relies on the distinction between identity (huwiyya) and form (ṣūra).
3
Grasp the concluding argument
Read the final part where the author states that knowledge of non-existents necessitates mental existence, making it self-evident.
This concludes the defense of mental existence.

قال: ويستدل على تحقق الذهني (١) : بأن نحكم إيجاباً على ما لا ثبوت له ، في الخارج، كالممتنعات (۲) مع استحالة الإثبات لما لا ثبوت له، وبأن نجد من المفهومات ما هو كلي يمتنع بكليته في الخارج. ومن القضايا حقيقية لا يقتصر الحكم فيها على الموجود في الخارج، واعترض بأنه يكفي في الإيجاب تميز الموضوع عند العقل، وهو معنى التعقل فيرجع الكل إلى أن الفهم والتعقل، يقتضي الثبوت في العقل وفيه النزاع . والجواب : أن اقتضاء التعقل والتميز إضافة بين العاقل والمعقول ضروري ولا تعقل الإضافة إلى النفي السرف، بل لا بد من ثبوت (ما) وإذ ليس في الخارج ففي العقل . فإن قيل : يجوز أن يقوم بنفسه ، كالمثل المجردة (۳) لأفلاطون والمعلقة لغيره، أو ببعض المجردات كصور الكائنات بالعقل الفعال عند الفلاسفة قلنا : معلوم بالضرورة أن الممتنع (٤) بل المعدوم سيما (٥) ما ليس من قبيل الذوات لا يقوم بنفسه، ولا ببعض المجردات بهويته بل بصورته، وفيه المدعي

The Second Proof for Mental Existence
Author presents the second proof for mental existence using affirmative judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second proof for mental existence works and how it avoids the charge of circularity.

Core Idea
Affirmative judgments about impossible things demand their existence in the mind.

This passage presents a key argument for the existence of mental objects. The author shows that we make true affirmative judgments about things that have no external reality, like the properties of impossibilities. Such judgments require the subject to exist in some sense, and since not externally, they must exist mentally. The author also defends the argument against the objection of circularity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the examples used.
2
Identify the proof
The author presents a proof from affirmative judgments. See how he uses examples like 'the conjunction of two contradictories is necessary to each.'
3
Understand the objection
The objection is that the argument assumes mental existence (begging the question). The author responds by clarifying that the proof only requires existence in some respect.
This is a common misunderstanding.
4
Appreciate the self-evident conclusion
The author concludes that the proof is self-evident (badīhī). Reflect on why he says that.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستدل المثبتون بوجوه . الأول : أنا نحكم حكماً إيجابياً على ما لا تحقق له في الخارج أصلاً، كقولنا : اجتماع النقيضين مستلزم لكل منهما، ومغاير لاجتماع الضدين، ونحو ذلك. ومعنى الإيجاب الحكم بثبوت أمر لأمر، وثبوت الشيء لما لا ثبوت له في الاستحالة، فيلزم ثبوت الممتنعات لتصح هذه الأحكام، وإذ ليس في الخارج نفي الذهن وتقرير آخر أن من الموجبات ما لا تحقق (۳) لموضوعه في الخارج والموجبة تستدعي وجود الموضوع في الجملة فيكون في الذهن. وما يقال إنا نحكم على الممتنعات بأحكام ،ثبوتية، فمعناه أحكام إيجابية فلا يرد عليه : أنه إن أريد الثبوت في الخارج فمحال أو في الذهن فمصادرة (4) على أنه يجوز أن يقال المراد الثبوت في الجملة وكونه منحصراً في الخارجي والذهني لا يستلزم أن يراد أحدهما ليلزم المحال والمصادرة . نفسه بديهي
And the affirmers adduced proofs. The first: that we give an affirmative judgment about that which has no realization in the external world at all, like our saying: the conjunction of two contradictories is necessary to each of them, and it is different from the conjunction of two opposites, and so on. The meaning of affirmation (ījāb) is the judgment of the existence (thubūt) of something for something; and the existence of a thing for that which has no existence is impossible. So the existence of impossible things is entailed (to make these judgments true), and since it is not in the external world, [it must be] in the mind. And another formulation: among affirmative judgments are those whose subject has no realization in the external world, and the affirmative proposition requires the existence of the subject in some respect (fī al-jumla), so it is in the mind. And what is said: that we judge impossible things with affirmative judgments, its meaning is that they are affirmative judgments; so it is not subject to the objection that if external existence is intended, it is impossible, or if mental existence is intended, it is begging the question (muṣādara). Rather, it may be said that by existence is meant existence in some respect, and its being restricted to external and mental does not entail that one of them is intended such that the impossible and begging the question would follow. It is self-evident.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for mental existence. The author states that the proponents (al-muthbitūn) have several proofs, and the first of them is that we make affirmative judgments about things that have no external existence at all, such as the proposition 'the conjunction of two contradictories is necessary to each' and 'it is different from the conjunction of two opposites.' The meaning of affirmation is to judge that something exists for something (thubūt shay’ li-shay’). For a judgment to be true, the subject must have some mode of existence, otherwise the attribution would be impossible. Since the subjects of these judgments have no external existence, they must exist mentally. The author then provides another formulation: among affirmative propositions (al-mūjibāt) are those whose subject has no realization externally, yet the affirmative proposition demands the existence of the subject in some respect (wujūd al-mawḍū‘ fī al-jumla). Consequently, the subject must exist in the mind. The author then addresses a potential objection: one might say that if by 'existence' we mean external existence, then the judgment about impossibilities would be impossible; if we mean mental existence, then the argument assumes what it is trying to prove (muṣādara). The author refutes this by stating that the intended meaning is existence in some respect (al-thubūt fī al-jumla), and that existence is restricted to external and mental. It does not follow that we must intend one of them specifically in such a way that the impossibility or petition would occur. The author concludes that the proof is self-evident (badīhī).

Key Terms
وجود ذهني (wujūd dhihnī)
ممتنع (mumtani‘)
إيجاب (ījāb)
ثبوت (thubūt)
مصادرة (muṣādara)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second proof for mental existence is circular (muṣādara) because it assumes mental existence.
Correction: The author clarifies that the proof only demands existence in some respect (fī al-jumla), not specifically mental existence; mental existence is inferred because external existence is impossible for the subjects. Thus it is not begging the question.

Checkpoint
What is the key response to the charge of begging the question (muṣādara) in the second proof?
A) The proof is based on revelation.
B) The proof only requires existence in some respect, not specifically mental existence.
C) The proof is accepted by all philosophers.
D) The proof is self-evident.

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that our true affirmative judgments about impossible things require the subject to exist mentally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You affirm things in your mind every day—ideas about God, about right and wrong, about your own existence. But do you ever pause to consider that even your false or contradictory thoughts have a kind of existence? If every thought you entertain has mental being, what does that mean for the value you place on your inner life? Will you today consciously purify your mental world, knowing it is a real realm in which you dwell before Allah?
?
The proof says that even judgments about impossibles—like 'a square circle'—require a subject that exists mentally. If your mind can hold and reason about impossibilities, how much more real are your beliefs about Allah and His attributes? Are you treating your convictions as merely abstract ideas, or as realities that shape your existence? Will you examine one belief you hold about Allah and ask: 'Is this belief truly present in my mind as a living reality, or is it just a phrase?'

The First Proof for Mental Existence
First proof for mental existence from judgments on non-existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof for mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni) and how it is derived from judgments about impossible things.

Core Idea
Positive judgments force non-existents to exist in the mind.

This lesson introduces the first and most direct proof for mental existence from the activity of judgment. It argues that when we make affirmative statements about things that do not exist externally, such as 'the conjunction of opposites is impossible,' we must be referring to something that exists — not in the external world, but in the mind. This proof is central to the discussion and sets the stage for further arguments. The passage also addresses a potential objection that the proof is circular, and shows why it is not.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the proof.
Pay attention to the objection and response sections.
2
Identify the core claim
The proof begins with a claim: denying mental existence is to deny something self-evident. Notice why.
This claim is supported by the subsequent argument.
3
Understand how the proof works
The proof uses positive judgments about non-existent subjects to show that those subjects must exist in the mind. Understand the reasoning: a positive judgment requires the subject to exist in some sense; external reality doesn’t have it, so it must be mental.
The key move is from 'existence in some sense' to 'mental existence'.
4
Consider the objection and response
An objector might say the proof is circular because it assumes mental existence. Read how the response clarifies that the existence required is general (al-thubut fi al-jumla), not specifically mental, thus avoiding circularity.
This shows the proof is not begging the question.
5
Reflect on the importance
Think about why this proof is foundational for the doctrine of mental existence. It ties logic to metaphysics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمن هنا زعم بعضهم أن إنكار الوجود الذهني إنكار للأمر الضروري واستدل المثبتون بوجوه . الأول : أنا نحكم حكماً إيجابياً على ما لا تحقق له في الخارج أصلاً، كقولنا : اجتماع النقيضين مستلزم لكل منهما، ومغاير لاجتماع الضدين، ونحو ذلك. ومعنى الإيجاب الحكم بثبوت أمر لأمر، وثبوت الشيء لما لا ثبوت له في الاستحالة، فيلزم ثبوت الممتنعات لتصح هذه الأحكام، وإذ ليس في الخارج نفي الذهن وتقرير آخر أن من الموجبات ما لا تحقق (۳) لموضوعه في الخارج والموجبة تستدعي وجود الموضوع في الجملة فيكون في الذهن. وما يقال إنا نحكم على الممتنعات بأحكام ثبوتية، فمعناه أحكام إيجابية فلا يرد عليه : أنه إن أريد الثبوت في الخارج فمحال أو في الذهن فمصادرة (4) على أنه يجوز أن يقال المراد الثبوت في الجملة وكونه منحصراً في الخارجي والذهني لا يستلزم أن يراد أحدهما ليلزم المحال والمصادرة .
So some claimed that denying mental existence is denying a self-evident matter, and the affirmers adduced proofs. The first: we make a positive judgment about what has no actuality in the external at all, like our statement: the conjunction of two opposites is necessary for each of them, and is different from the conjunction of two contraries, and the like. And the meaning of affirmation is judging the existence of a thing for a thing, and the existence of a thing for that which has no existence is impossible, so it necessitates the existence of impossible things for these judgments to be correct, and since [the subject] is not in the external, [it necessitates] affirming the mind. Another formulation: there are affirmative [propositions] whose subject has no actuality in the external, and the affirmative proposition requires the existence of the subject in some sense, so it is in the mind. And what is said—that we make judgments about impossible things with affirmative predicates, so their meaning is affirmative judgments—does not refute [the proof], because if external existence is meant, it is impossible, and if mental existence is meant, it is a petitio principii, except that it may be said: the intended [existence] is existence in some sense, and its being confined to external and mental does not necessitate that one of them is intended, such that impossibility or petitio principii would follow.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni) from judgments about non-existent things. It begins by citing a claim that denying mental existence is to deny something self-evident. Then it states the proof: We make positive judgments (ahkam ijabiyya) about things that have no external reality, such as 'the conjunction of opposites is necessary for each of them' or 'it is different from the conjunction of contraries'. A positive judgment means judging the existence of a predicate for a subject. For such a judgment to be valid, the subject must have some sort of existence; otherwise, affirming something of nothing is impossible. Since the subject (e.g., the conjunction of opposites) has no external existence, it must exist in the mind. The passage then considers an objection: One might say that our judgments about impossibles are merely affirmative in wording, not in reality. But the response is that the judgment is indeed affirmative, and if we say the subject exists externally it is false, if we say it exists mentally it is a petitio principii (because we are trying to prove mental existence). However, the response further clarifies that we mean existence in some sense (al-thubut fi al-jumla), which is not restricted to external or mental exclusively, so the dilemma is avoided.

Key Terms
الوجود الذهني (al-wujud al-dhihni)
حكم إيجابي (positive judgment)
ثبوت (existence/establishment)
ممتنعات (impossibles)
مصادرة (petitio principii)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is circular because it assumes mental existence to prove mental existence.
Correction: The proof does not assume mental existence; it requires only that the subject have existence in some general sense. That general existence is then shown to be mental because external existence is impossible for such subjects.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why must the subject of a positive judgment about an impossible thing exist in the mind?
A) Because the subject has external existence.
B) Because a positive judgment requires the subject to exist in some sense, and since it has no external existence, it must exist mentally.
C) Because the objector concedes mental existence.
D) Because all impossible things are concepts.

Key Takeaway
The first proof for mental existence demonstrates that positive judgments about non-existent things require those subjects to exist in the mind, and this reasoning withstands the objection of circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the attributes of Allah, do you ever fall into the trap of assuming that your mental image of them is the same as their external reality? How does this proof force you to rethink that assumption?
?
The proof requires that even impossible things have a type of existence in the mind. How does this affect your understanding of the reality of your own thoughts? Are you aware that every thought you have, even about falsehoods, has a mental existence for which you are accountable?
?
If positive judgments about non-existents necessitate mental existence, what does that imply about the reality of the judgments you make about Allah? Are you careful to ensure that your judgments are based on revelation, not just mental constructs?

The Second Proof: Universals Exist in the Mind
Second proof: universals are mental concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the universal concept serves as a proof for mental existence.

Core Idea
Universals exist in the mind because they are concepts distinguished in intellect, not in external particular things.

After establishing the first proof (not in this passage), Sanusi presents the second argument for mental existence based on universals. The universal, such as 'human' or 'animal', is a concept that the mind grasps. But universals are not found in the external world, where everything is particular. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the second proof
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the logical structure.
The proof is very concise.
2
Identify the claim about universals
Identify what Sanusi claims about the universal: it is a concept, established in the intellect by distinction.
3
Follow the reasoning
Understand the inference: because external things are particular, the universal cannot be external; therefore it must be mental.
This is a direct argument from the nature of universals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن الكلي ،مفهوم ، وكل مفهوم ثابت ضرورة تميزه عند العقل . فالكلي ثابت وليس في الخارج، لأن كل ما هو في الخارج مشخص فيكون في الذهن. الثالث : إن من القضايا موجبة حقيقية، وهي تستدعي وجود الموضوع ضرورة، وليس في الخارج لأنه قد لا يوجد في الخارج أصلا، كقولنا : كل عنقاء حيوان ، " وعلى تقدير الوجود لا تنحصر الأحكام في الأفراد الخارجية، كقولنا : كل جسم متناه، أو حادث، أو مركب (۱) من أجزاء لا تتجزأ، إلى غير ذلك من المستعملة في العلوم ، فالحكم على جميع الأفراد لا يكون إلا باعتبار الوجود في الذهن وفي المواقف ما يشعر بأن قولنا : الممتنع معدوم قضية حقيقية. وليس كذلك في اصطلاح القوم
The second: the universal is a concept, and every concept is established necessarily by its distinction in the intellect. So the universal is established and is not in the external, because everything in the external is particular, so it is in the mind. The third: among propositions are true affirmative ones, and they require the existence of the subject necessarily, and [the subject] is not in the external because it may not exist in the external at all, like our statement: every phoenix is an animal. " And with the assumption of existence, judgments are not confined to external individuals, like our statement: every body is finite, or originated, or composed of indivisible parts, and other such [propositions] used in the sciences. So judgment on all individuals is only with consideration of existence in the mind. And in al-Mawaqif there is a suggestion that our statement 'the impossible is nonexistent' is a true proposition, but it is not so in the terminology of the people.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage presents the second and third proofs for mental existence. The second proof argues from universals (al-kulli): The universal is a concept (mafhum), and every concept is established (thabit) in the intellect because it is distinguished (tamyiz) there. But universals do not exist in the external world because everything external is particular (mushakhkhas). Therefore, universals must exist in the mind. The third proof relies on true affirmative propositions (qadaya mujiba haqiqiyya). Such propositions require the existence of the subject for their truth. However, often the subject has no external existence (e.g., 'every phoenix is an animal'), so the subject must exist mentally. Moreover, judgments that apply to all individuals of a kind (e.g., 'every body is finite') cannot be made solely by considering external individuals, because those individuals are many and not all present; rather, the judgment is made on the mental concept of the kind. Thus, mental existence is necessary. The passage also notes a remark from al-Mawaqif that the proposition 'the impossible is nonexistent' might be considered a true affirmative proposition, but this is not the accepted terminology.

Key Terms
الكلي (universal)
مفهوم (concept)
ثابت (established)
تميز (distinction)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That universals exist as real entities in the external world.
Correction: The proof shows universals are mental concepts, not external particulars.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, why must universals exist in the mind?
A) Because they are perceived by the senses.
B) Because they are concepts distinguished in the intellect and external things are particular.
C) Because they are impossible externally.
D) Because they are eternal.

Key Takeaway
Universals are mental concepts, not external particulars, proving mental existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of concepts like 'justice' or 'mercy', do you treat them as mere words or as real mental entities established in your intellect by Allah's design? How might recognizing their mental reality change your commitment to living them?
?
If everything external is particular, including your own body, but the concept of 'soul' is universal, what does that imply about the nature of your soul and its existence beyond the physical?
?
Sanusi says concepts are established 'by distinction in the intellect'. Have you ever actively distinguished between your mental concepts and the external objects they represent? What did that distinction reveal about your reliance on intellectual clarity?

The Third Proof: True Propositions Require Mental Existence
Third proof: true affirmative propositions require mental existence of their subjects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how true affirmative propositions demonstrate the necessity of mental existence.

Core Idea
True propositions about non-existent subjects prove that subjects exist mentally.

The third proof extends the argument by analyzing the nature of true affirmative propositions. Some such propositions have subjects that do not exist externally, yet the proposition is true. This forces us to admit that the subject exists mentally. Additionally, even for existing subjects, universal judgments range over all individuals, which requires a mental concept of the kind. This lesson explores these points.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the third proof
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the two sub-arguments and the final remark.
2
Understand the first strand: subjects with no external existence
Look at the example 'every phoenix is an animal'. Phoenix does not exist externally, yet the proposition is true. This implies the subject exists mentally.
3
Understand the second strand: universal judgments
Examine the example 'every body is finite'. Such judgments apply to all individuals, not just present ones, requiring a mental concept.
4
Consider the remark from al-Mawaqif
Note the comment about 'the impossible is nonexistent' and why it is not considered a true proposition in standard terminology.
This is a minor point but shows engagement with other views.

Objection and Answer on Mental Existence
Objection to proofs and redefinition of affirmation as mental distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection raised against the proofs of mental existence and how the response clarifies that judgment requires only mental distinction, not external existence.

Core Idea
Affirmation does not need external existence, only mental distinction.

This passage presents a critical objection to the earlier proofs that affirmation (positive judgment) requires the existence of the subject. The objector argues that we do not concede that affirmation necessitates the subject's existence. In response, the text redefines affirmation as mere conceptual correspondence, requiring only distinction in the intellect. This leads to the very point of contention: whether conceptualization entails mental existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the objector's claim: 'We do not concede that affirmation necessitates the existence of the subject.' Identify the specific point of disagreement.
The objector is challenging a premise used in the three proofs.
2
Understand the redefinition of affirmation
The answer redefines affirmation: it is not about the existence of the predicate in the subject, but merely about the conceptual distinction and correspondence between subject and predicate in the intellect.
This shift from external existence to mental distinction is key.
3
Trace the implication to the core dispute
The text then states that this redefinition reduces the three proofs to the claim that we conceive things without external existence—which is exactly the point of contention. Notice how the answer is cut off but sets up a further response.
The passage ends mid-sentence, indicating the answer continues on the next page.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض بأنا : لا نسلم أن الايجاب يقتضي وجود الموضوع ، قولكم (۲) إن ثبوت الشيء للشيء فرع ثبوته في نفسه . قولنا : معنى الإيجاب أن (۳) ما صدق عليه الموضوع، هو ما صدق عليه المحمول من غير أن يكون هناك ثبوت أمر الأمر بمعنى الوجود والتحقق فيه، وإنما ذلك بحسب العبارة، وعلى اعتبار الوجود الذهني، بل اللازم هو تميز الموضوع، والمحمول عند العقل بمعنى تصورهما، فيكون مرجع الوجوه الثلاثة : إلى أنا نتصور ونفهم أموراً لا وجود لها في الخارج، فتكون ثابتة في الذهن لأن تعقل الشيء إنما يكون بحصوله في العقل بصورته، إن كان من الموجودات العينية وإلا فبنفسه (٤) ، وهذا نفس المتنازع . لأن القول بكون التعقل بالحصول في العقل إنما هو رأي القائلين بالوجود الذهني، وإلا لكان بشيء ما كافياً في إثبات المطلوب. والجواب : أنه لا بد في فهم الشيء وتعقله وتمييزه عند العقل من تعلق بين
And an objection is made: We do not concede that affirmation necessitates the existence of the subject. Your statement that the existence of a thing for a thing is a branch of its own existence [is not accepted]. We say: The meaning of affirmation is that what the subject applies to is what the predicate applies to, without there being an existence of a thing for a thing in the sense of being and actuality therein; rather, that is according to expression and with consideration of mental existence. Rather, what is necessary is the distinction of the subject and predicate in the intellect, meaning their conceptualization. Thus the reference of the three proofs is that we conceptualize and understand things that have no existence in the external, so they are established in the mind because understanding a thing is only by its form obtaining in the intellect—if it is among external existents, [its form obtains]; otherwise, it obtains by itself. And this is the very point of contention, because the statement that understanding is by obtaining in the intellect is only the view of those who affirm mental existence; otherwise, something else would suffice to prove the required. And the answer: it is necessary in understanding a thing and conceptualizing it and distinguishing it in the intellect that there be a relation between ...
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the three proofs for mental existence and the beginning of the answer. The objector says: We do not accept that affirmation (al-ijab) requires the existence (wujud) of the subject. The claim that 'the existence of a thing for a thing is a branch of its own existence' is questioned. The answer redefines affirmation: it merely means that the subject and predicate apply to the same thing, not that there is a separate existence of the predicate in the subject. Instead, what is needed is the distinction (tamyiz) of the subject and predicate in the intellect, i.e., their conceptualization. Therefore, the three proofs really reduce to the fact that we conceive things that have no external existence, so they are established in the mind. But this is the very point of dispute, because the theory that conceptualization occurs by the form of the thing obtaining in the intellect is only held by those who affirm mental existence; otherwise, something else would suffice as proof. The answer then begins by saying that understanding and conceptualization require a relation (ta'alliq) between the intellect and the thing, but the sentence is cut off. The incomplete answer suggests that the necessity of such a relation is not disputed, but the full resolution is not provided in this window.

Key Terms
إيجاب (affirmation)
وجود (existence)
تميز (distinction)
تصور (conceptualization)
تعقل (intellection)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection successfully refutes the proofs of mental existence.
Correction: The answer shows that the objection only forces a clarification: affirmation requires mental distinction, not external existence, and this actually supports the concept of mental existence.

Checkpoint
How does the answer respond to the objection that affirmation does not require the existence of the subject?
A) By insisting that affirmation requires external existence.
B) By stating that affirmation only requires the mental distinction of subject and predicate, not their separate existence.
C) By rejecting the objection as false.
D) By saying that the subject must exist in the mind exactly as it exists externally.

Key Takeaway
The objection forces a clarification: affirmation does not prove external existence but only mental distinction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold that has no external reality—perhaps an opinion about the future or a concept like justice. How does this lesson's claim that affirmation requires only mental distinction, not external existence, challenge you to examine whether your belief is truly grounded or merely a mental construct? Will you test it against reality today?
?
The objector tried to avoid affirming mental existence, but the answer shows that even simple judgment involves mental conceptualization. In your daily life, how often do you make judgments (about people, ideas, or plans) without considering that they exist only in your mind? Are you willing to admit that your certainty about those judgments may rest on mental existence alone? What would it cost you to reevaluate one such judgment?
?
This lesson forces a distinction between external existence and mental conceptualization. Consider an idea you passionately defend—does its truth depend on external reality, or on your mental coherence? If it were proven to have no external counterpart, would you still hold it? How would your attachment to that idea change? Resolve to identify one such idea today and seek its grounding.

The Third Point: Subject Existence in Affirmative Propositions
Taftazani states the third point: affirmative propositions require mental existence of the subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Taftazani's claim that true affirmative propositions require the mental existence of the subject, even if the subject does not exist externally.

Core Idea
Affirmative judgments about non-existent subjects are true because the subject exists in the mind.

Taftazani begins his third argument by asserting that true affirmative propositions necessarily require the existence of the subject. However, this existence is not external—subjects like 'phoenix' or 'body' may not exist in the outside world, or judgments cover all individuals, including imaginary ones. This lesson introduces the claim and its justification.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim passage
Read Taftazani's third point carefully. Notice how he introduces the need for existence of the subject.
Pay attention to the examples: phoenix, body, etc.
2
Identify the two types of examples
He gives examples of non-existent subjects (phoenix) and existing subjects where judgments cover all individuals (body). Both require mental existence.
3
Connect to the need for mental existence
Reflect on why only mental existence can account for these judgments, since external existence is not always present.
This is the core claim of the lesson.
4
Note the reference to al-Mawaqif
Taftazani mentions that in al-Mawaqif a similar point is made, but it differs in terminology.
This sets up the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن من القضايا موجبة حقيقية، وهي تستدعي وجود الموضوع ضرورة، وليس في الخارج لأنه قد لا يوجد في الخارج أصلا، كقولنا : كل عنقاء حيوان، وعلى تقدير الوجود لا تنحصر الأحكام في الأفراد الخارجية، كقولنا : كل جسم متناه، أو حادث، أو مركب من أجزاء لا تتجزأ، إلى غير ذلك من المستعملة في العلوم، فالحكم على جميع الأفراد لا يكون إلا باعتبار الوجود في الذهن وفي المواقف ما يشعر بأن قولنا : الممتنع معدوم قضية حقيقية. وليس كذلك في اصطلاح القوم واعترض بأنا : لا نسلم أن الايجاب يقتضي وجود الموضوع، قولكم إن ثبوت الشيء للشيء فرع ثبوته في نفسه. قلنا : معنى الإيجاب أن ما صدق عليه الموضوع، هو ما صدق عليه المحمول من غير أن يكون هناك ثبوت أمر الأمر بمعنى الوجود والتحقق فيه، وإنما ذلك بحسب العبارة، وعلى اعتبار الوجود الذهني، بل اللازم هو تميز الموضوع، والمحمول عند العقل بمعنى تصورهما، فيكون مرجع الوجوه الثلاثة : إلى أنا نتصور ونفهم أموراً لا وجود لها في الخارج، فتكون ثابتة في الذهن لأن تعقل الشيء إنما يكون بحصوله في العقل بصورته، إن كان من الموجودات العينية وإلا فبنفسه، وهذا نفس المتنازع. لأن القول بكون التعقل بالحصول في العقل إنما هو رأي القائلين بالوجود الذهني، وإلا لكان بشيء ما كافياً في إثبات المطلوب. والجواب : أنه لا بد في فهم الشيء وتعقله وتمييزه عند العقل من تعلق بين العاقل والمعقول، سواء كان العلم عبارة عن حصول صورة الشيء في العقل عن إضافة مخصوصة بين العاقل والمعقول أو عن صفة ذات إضافة، والتعلق بين العاقل وبين العدم الصرف محال بالضرورة. فلا بد للمعقول من ثبوت في الجملة ولما امتنع ثبوت الكليات بل سائر المعدومات سيما الممتنعات في الخارج تعين كونه في الذهن.
Third: Among propositions are true affirmative ones, which necessarily require the existence of the subject, and not in the external world because it may not exist externally at all, like our saying: Every phoenix is an animal; and given existence, judgments are not restricted to external individuals, like our saying: Every body is finite, or originated, or composed of indivisible parts, and so on from what is used in the sciences. So judgment about all individuals is only by considering existence in the mind. And in al-Mawaqif it indicates that our saying: The impossible is non-existent is a true proposition. But it is not so in the terminology of the people. And it was objected: We do not grant that affirmation necessitates existence of the subject, [by] your saying that the existence of a thing for a thing is dependent on its existence in itself. We said: The meaning of affirmation is that what the subject applies to is what the predicate applies to, without there being existence of the attribute in the sense of being and realization in it; rather that is according to expression and on the consideration of mental existence. Rather what is necessary is the distinction of the subject and the predicate in the mind, meaning their conceptualization. So the reference of the three aspects is that we conceptualize and understand things that have no existence in the external world, so they are established in the mind, because the intellect of a thing is only by its form obtaining in the mind, if it is among external existents, otherwise by itself. And this is the very point of dispute, because the saying that intellection is by the form obtaining in the mind is only the view of those who affirm mental existence; otherwise something would be sufficient to prove the claim. And the answer: It is necessary in understanding a thing, intellection, and its distinction in the mind for there to be a relation between the intellect and the intelligible, whether knowledge is defined as the obtaining of the form of a thing in the mind by a specific relation between the intellect and the intelligible, or as an attribute possessing relation. And the relation between the intellect and pure non-existence is impossible necessarily. So the intelligible must have some existence; and since the existence of universals, and indeed all non-existents especially impossibilities, is impossible externally, it is determined to be in the mind.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument combining a claim, an objection, and two answers. The author (Taftazani) presents the third point: true affirmative propositions require the existence of the subject. However, this existence is not external—subjects like 'phoenix' or 'body' may not exist externally or the judgment covers all individuals even if not all are external. So the subject exists mentally. An objection is raised: affirmation does not require existence, because the principle 'the existence of a thing for a thing is dependent on its existence in itself' is not conceded. The first answer (قلنا) redefines affirmation: it only requires that the subject and predicate are conceptually distinguished in the mind, not external existence. This is the very point at issue—those who deny mental existence would not accept this. The second answer (والجواب) provides a stronger argument: intellection requires a relation between knower and known; relation to pure non-existence is impossible; therefore the intelligible must have some existence; since it cannot be external, it must be mental.

Key Terms
القضايا الموجبة
وجود الموضوع
الوجود الذهني
المواقف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Affirmative propositions require the subject to exist in the external world.
Correction: Taftazani shows that subjects like 'phoenix' do not exist externally, yet we make true affirmative judgments about them, so the subject must exist mentally.

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani's third point, why must the subject exist in the mind?
A) Because all subjects exist externally.
B) Because external existence is impossible.
C) Because judgments cover all individuals including non-existent ones.
D) Because the predicate requires it.

Key Takeaway
True affirmative propositions require the subject to exist in the mind, not necessarily in external reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Taftazani argues that even judgments about non-existent or impossible things have truth-value because they exist in the mind. When you reflect on your own beliefs about the unseen—like the soul, angels, or the afterlife—do you treat them as mental concepts or as real external existences? How does this lesson challenge you to examine the foundation of your faith claims?
?
Consider a time you made a broad statement about a group of people or a situation you had never directly experienced. How did your mind 'create' the subject of that judgment? Are you willing to admit that many of your opinions are based on mental constructs rather than concrete external realities? What step could you take today to ground your judgments in more direct observation or reliable testimony?
?
If the impossible (al-mumtani') can be the subject of a true proposition, then your own mind constantly deals with ideas that have no external counterpart. How does that humble your confidence in your own reasoning? In what area of your life might you be confusing mental existence with external truth, and how can you correct that starting now?
